Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
Two Meanings of Tawhid and the Book's Structure
The author defines two interpretations of 'Allah is one' and divides the book accordingly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct theological meanings of saying 'Allah is one' and how they determine the entire structure of this book.

Core Idea
Tawhid means two things: no parts and no rivals.

The author begins by unpacking the rich meaning of the simple phrase 'Allah is one.' This seemingly straightforward statement carries two distinct theological claims, each requiring its own proof. Understanding these two interpretations is essential because they determine how the entire book is organized.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the clear two-part structure the author announces.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two interpretations of 'Allah is one.' Find each one and note what they negate: the first negates composition, the second negates opposites and equals.
Each interpretation corresponds to a section of the book.
3
Connect to the book's layout
The author explicitly states that the book is divided into two sections based on these two meanings. See how the first section covers spatial transcendence and the second covers transcendence from rivals.
This is a key methodological insight: the book's structure mirrors the content of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم. تمهيد: اعلم أنا إذا قلنا: الله واحد، فله تفسيران: أحدهما: أن ذاته ليست مركبة من الأجزاء والأبعاض. وذلك لا يتم إلا ببيان أنه تعالى ليس بمتحيز، ولا في جهة. والثاني: بيان أنه سبحانه منزه عن الضد والند. ولهذا السبب ترتب هذا الكتاب على قسمين: القسم الأول: في بيان كونه سبحانه وتعالى منزها عن التحيز والجهة. والقسم الثاني: في بيانه أنه سبحانه منزه عن الضد والند.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Introduction: Know that when we say: Allah is one, it has two interpretations: One: that His essence is not composed of parts and limbs. And that is not complete except by explaining that He, exalted, is not occupying space, nor in a direction. The second: the explanation that He, glorified, is transcendent above the opposite and the equal. And for this reason, this book is arranged into two sections: The first section: concerning the explanation that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above occupying space and direction. And the second section: concerning His explanation that He, glorified, is transcendent above the opposite and the equal.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is both a definition and a classification. The author begins by stating that the statement 'Allah is one' carries two distinct interpretations. The first interpretation defines tawhid as the negation of composition: Allah's essence (dhat) is not made up of parts or limbs. This, the author notes, cannot be fully established without also affirming that Allah is not a body occupying space (tahayyuz) and is not located in any direction (jiha). The second interpretation defines tawhid as the negation of any opposite (did) or equal (nadd) to Allah. On the basis of these two meanings, the author then explains the structure of the entire work: the first section addresses the transcendence of Allah beyond spatiality and directionality (i.e., the first interpretation), and the second section addresses His transcendence beyond any opponent or rival (i.e., the second interpretation). Thus the book's division follows directly from the twofold meaning of tawhid itself.

Key Terms
تفسيران (two interpretations)
تحيز (occupying space)
جهة (direction)
ضد (opposite)
ند (equal)
أجزاء (parts)
أبعاض (limbs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Allah is one' only means numerical oneness, i.e., that there is only one God.
Correction: The passage shows that 'one' here also means that Allah's essence is not composed of parts, and that He has no opposite or equal. These are deeper theological claims beyond mere number.

Checkpoint
What are the two interpretations of 'Allah is one' according to this passage?
A) Negation of composition and negation of attributes.
B) Negation of spatial occupancy and direction, and negation of opposite and equal.
C) Affirmation of oneness and denial of multiplicity.
D) Unity of essence and unity of actions.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'Allah is one' encompasses both the negation of composition and the negation of partners, which together shape the entire discussion of divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is one,' have you ever considered that this means Allah has no parts and is not composed? How does this understanding affect the way you think about Allah's attributes? Take a moment to examine your own concept of Allah's oneness: do you truly affirm His simplicity, or do you unknowingly imagine a being with parts?
?
If tawhid includes both rejecting composition and rejecting rivals, which of these two aspects is more neglected in your own beliefs and worship? Will you commit to studying both dimensions so that your tawhid is complete?
?
The author structured his entire book on these two interpretations. In your own life, do you give equal attention to negating what is unworthy of Allah and negating any rivals? How can you adjust your daily dhikr or study to reflect both aspects?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
4 min
The Core Argument: Man as Abstract Concept
The author presents the initial proof for divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the shared meaning of 'man' among individuals proves that the concept is abstract and why this makes divine transcendence rationally plausible.

Core Idea
If 'man' is an abstract intelligible, the Creator can be even more so.

The third proof begins with a simple observation: all humans share a common meaning of 'manhood' despite their differences. This reveals that the concept of man is an abstract intelligible, not tied to any specific shape or place. If even a human concept can be non-sensory, then it is not unreasonable to believe that the Creator of all things is utterly transcendent beyond sensory attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the self-evident premise
Read the first sentence: people share a common meaning of 'man' but differ in particulars. Note that this is presented as known by necessity.
The author takes this as given, not argued.
2
Distinguish shared from differential
Recognize that what makes them similar (the shared meaning) is different from what makes them unique. This logical distinction is key.
3
Draw the conclusion about 'man'
The passage concludes that 'man' as such is an abstract concept, free from specific form and place. Understand why this follows.
If the shared meaning were tied to a particular form, it couldn't apply to all.
4
Apply to the Creator
The author then asks: if man is abstract, why would it be improbable for the Creator to be even more transcendent? Reflect on the rhetorical force.
The argument does not prove the Creator exists, but removes an objection to His transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن أشخاص الناس مشتركة في المعنى المفهوم من قولنا: إنسان ومتباينة بتعيناتها وخصوصياتها(1) وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة .. وهذا يدل على أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان : مفهوم مجرد عن الشكل المعين ، والحيز المعين. فثبت أن الإنسان معقول مفهوم مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المخلوقات (2) منزها عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال ؟ (1) قال ابن سينا في : الإشارات والتنبيهات، في النمط الرابع من الإلهيات : اعلم : أنه قد يغلب على أوهام الناس : أن الموجود هو المحسوس ، وأن ما لا يناله الحس ففرض وجوده محال . وأن ما لا يتخصص بمكان أو وضع بذاته كالجسم ، أو لسبب ما هو فيه كأحوال الجسم فلا حظ له من الوجود . وأنت بتأن لك أن تتأمل نفس المحسوس ، فتعلم منه بطلان قول هؤلاء ، لأنك - ومن يستحق أن يخاطب - تعلمان أن هذه المحسوسات قد يقع عليها اسم واحد . لا على سبيل الاشتراك الصرف ، بل بحسب معنى واحد . مثل اسم الإنسان . فإنكما لا تشكان في أن وقوعه على زيد وعمرو ، بمعنى واحد : موجود . وذلك المعنى الموجود ، لا يخلو إما أن يكون بحيث يناله الحس، أو لا يكون . فإن كان بعيدا من أن يناله الحس، فقد أخرج التفتيش عن المحسوسات : ما ليس بمحسوس . وهذا أعجب. وإن كان محسوسا لأنه لا محالة وضع وأين ومقدار معين وكيف معين ، لا يتأتى أن يحس ، بل ولا أن يتخيل إلا كذلك . فإن كل محسوس وكل متخيل ، فإنه يتخصص لا محالة لشيء من هذه الأحوال . وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن ملائما لما ليس بتلك الحال ، فلم يكن مقولا على كثيرين مختلفين في تلك الحال فإذن الإنسان من حيث هو واحد الحقيقة بل من حيث حقيقته الأصلية التي لا تختلف فيها الكثرة غير محسوس ، بل معقول صرف. وكذلك الحال في كل كلي. ولعل قائلا منهم يقول : إن الإنسان مثلا إنما هو إنسان من حيث له أعضاء ، من يد وعين وحاجب وغير ذلك . ومن حيث هو كذلك ، فهو محسوس ، فتنبه ونقول له : إن الحال في كل عضو كلي ، مما ذكرته أو تركته . كالحال في الإنسان نفسه .
تنبيه : إنه لو كان كل موجود بحيث يدخل في الوهم والحس. لكان الحس والوهم يدخلان في الحس والوهم، ولكان العقل الذي هو الحكم الحق يدخل في الوهم . ومن أبعد هذه الأصول، فليس شيء من العشق والخجل والوجل والغضب والشجاعة والجبن، مما يدخل في الحس والوهم، وهي من علائق الأمور المحسوسة . فما ظنك بموجودات ، إن كانت خارجة الذوات عن درجات المحسوسات وعلائقها ، أهـ (2) فثبت : أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان ، مفهوم مجرد عن . فقد أخرج التفتيش عن المحسوس ما هو معقول مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المحسوسات ... الخ (س) والعبارة مصححة
The third proof: we know by necessity that the individuals of people share in the meaning understood from our saying 'man' and differ by their specific determinations and particularities, and what is shared is other than what makes them different. And this indicates that man insofar as he is man is an abstract concept free from specific form and specific place. So it is established that man is an intelligible, abstract concept. And if this is the case, then how could it be considered improbable by the intellect that the Creator of creatures is transcendent above sensory accidents and imaginative attachments? (1) Ibn Sina said in al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat, in the fourth mode of the metaphysics: Know that it often dominates the fancies of people that existent is the sensed, and that what the sense does not reach, the supposition of its existence is impossible. And that what does not become particularized by a place or a position by itself like a body, or by a cause in which it is like the states of a body, has no share of existence. And you, by reflection, can contemplate the sensed itself and learn from it the falsity of these people's statement, because you and whoever deserves to be addressed both know that these sensed things may be named by one name, not merely by equivocation, but according to one meaning, like the name 'man'. For both of you do not doubt that its application to Zayd and 'Amr in one meaning is existent. And that existent meaning is not free from either being such that the sense attains it, or not. If it is far from being attained by sense, then the investigation has extracted from the sensed what is not sensed, and this is more astonishing. And if it is sensed, then necessarily it has a position, a where, a specific magnitude, and a specific quality; it cannot be sensed, nor even imagined, except thus. For every sensed and every imagined thing necessarily becomes particularized by something of these states. And if it is so, it would not be compatible with what is not in that state, so it would not be predicated of many differing in that state. Thus man insofar as he is one in reality, but rather by his original reality which does not differ in multiplicity, is not sensed but is a pure intelligible. And the same holds for every universal. And perhaps one of them may say: man, for example, is only man insofar as he has limbs, like hand, eye, eyebrow, etc., and insofar as he is thus, he is sensed. So pay attention and say to him: the case for each limb is a universal, whether you mentioned it or left it, like the case for man himself. 
Warning: If every existent were such that it enters into imagination and sense, then sense and imagination would enter into sense and imagination, and the intellect, which is the true judge, would enter into imagination. And the most remote of these principles: there is nothing of love, shame, fear, anger, courage, and cowardice that enters into sense and imagination, and they are attachments of sensed things. So what do you think of existents whose essences are outside the degrees of sensed things and their attachments? End of quote. (2) So it is established that man insofar as he is man is an abstract concept from. Indeed, the investigation has extracted from the sensed what is an abstract intelligible. And if this is the case, then how could it be considered improbable by the intellect that the Creator of sensed things... etc. The phrase is corrected.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the transcendence of God from Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah. The proof is structured in two parts: the author's own argument followed by a supporting quotation from Ibn Sina, and then a reassertion of the conclusion. The author begins by stating a self-evident observation: all individual humans share a common meaning when we say 'man', yet they differ in their particular determinations. This shows that what makes them similar and what makes them different are distinct. From this it follows that 'man' considered purely as such is an abstract concept, free from any specific shape or spatial location. Therefore, 'man' is an intelligible, abstract concept. If this is accepted, then why would reason deem it improbable that the Creator of all beings – including these abstract concepts – is utterly transcendent beyond sensory accidents (lawāḥiq al-ḥiss) and imaginative attachments (ʿalāʾiq al-khayāl)? The author then quotes Ibn Sina to reinforce the argument. Ibn Sina argues that people mistakenly think only sensible things exist, but the fact that a single meaning like 'man' applies to many distinct individuals proves that there is a meaning that is not purely sensible. He shows that if the common meaning were sensible, it would have to be particularized with position and magnitude, making it impossible for it to be predicated of many diverse individuals. Therefore, the common meaning is a pure intelligible (maʿqūl ṣirf), not a sensible. Ibn Sina extends the argument: even mental states like love and fear are not sensible, so how much more so must there be existents entirely outside the realm of sense? The author then restates the conclusion: since the investigation has extracted abstract intelligibles from sensed things, it is not improbable that the Creator of those sensed things is Himself abstract and transcendent. The commentary emphasizes that the proof does not claim that the abstract universal exists in the external world as a separate entity, but rather that the concept itself has a reality that is not dependent on sensory particularization, and this opens the door to affirming a transcendent Creator.

Key Terms
مفهوم مجرد
معقول
تنزيه
خالق
مشاركة
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof might be thought to endorse Platonic realism, where abstract concepts exist as separate entities in the external world.
Correction: The author does not claim that 'man' exists externally as a separate form; he only uses the logical fact that the common meaning is not reducible to sensory particulars, which opens the possibility for non-sensory realities like the Creator.

Checkpoint
What is the self-evident premise of the third proof?
A) All humans are identical in essence.
B) Humans share a meaning of 'man' but differ in particular determinations.
C) The concept 'man' is purely imaginary.
D) The Creator must be a body.

Key Takeaway
The shared meaning 'man' is an abstract intelligible, so the Creator can be even more abstract and transcendent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the concept of 'man' in your mind is abstract and free from specific form, then why do you insist on imagining God in human terms? What does your insistence reveal about your own attachment to sensory comfort?
?
You claim to believe in a transcendent Creator, yet most of your worship is tied to physical, tangible acts. How can you train your heart to connect with a Being who is beyond your imagination?
?
The proof shows that even ordinary concepts are non-sensory. Have you ever experienced the reality of an abstract concept like justice or love without reducing it to a physical form? How does that experience inform your understanding of God's transcendence?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
6 min
Ibn Sina's Support: The Intelligible Beyond Sense
Ibn Sina refutes the reductionist view and supports the abstract nature of universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that universal meanings like 'man' are not sensible, and how this strengthens the case for a transcendent Creator.

Core Idea
If universals are not sensible, then the Creator can be non-sensible.

To reinforce the proof, the author quotes Ibn Sina, who tackles the mistaken belief that only sensible things exist. Ibn Sina shows that the universal meaning of 'man' cannot be a particular sensible object because it applies to many different individuals. He also points out that emotions like love and fear are not sensible yet exist. This argument removes the prejudice that all existence must be physical, clearing the way for affirming a transcendent Creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the false assumption
Ibn Sina describes the common error: people think only what is sensed exists, and that anything non-sensory is impossible. Recognize this mindset.
This is called the 'sensible prejudice'.
2
Follow the argument from common names
Ibn Sina uses the name 'man' as an example. It applies to many individuals in one meaning. If that meaning were sensed, it would be particularized, so it couldn't be common. Therefore, it is not sensed.
This shows the meaning is an intelligible, not a sensible.
3
Consider potential objection and reply
An objector might say man is man because of limbs, which are sensed. Ibn Sina replies that each limb is itself a universal, so the problem recurs. Understand the regress.
4
Expand to non-sensible realities
Ibn Sina gives examples like love, fear, etc., which are not sensible but are real. This broadens the notion of existence beyond the physical.
If emotions can be non-sensible, why not the Creator?
5
Connect to the conclusion
The author restates the conclusion: the investigation has extracted abstract intelligibles from the sensed, so it is not improbable that the Creator of sensed things is abstract.
The proof does not prove God's existence, but removes an intellectual barrier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن أشخاص الناس مشتركة في المعنى المفهوم من قولنا: إنسان ومتباينة بتعيناتها وخصوصياتها(1) وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة .. وهذا يدل على أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان : مفهوم مجرد عن الشكل المعين ، والحيز المعين. فثبت أن الإنسان معقول مفهوم مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المخلوقات (2) منزها عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال ؟ (1) قال ابن سينا في : الإشارات والتنبيهات، في النمط الرابع من الإلهيات : اعلم : أنه قد يغلب على أوهام الناس : أن الموجود هو المحسوس ، وأن ما لا يناله الحس ففرض وجوده محال . وأن ما لا يتخصص بمكان أو وضع بذاته كالجسم ، أو لسبب ما هو فيه كأحوال الجسم فلا حظ له من الوجود . وأنت بتأن لك أن تتأمل نفس المحسوس ، فتعلم منه بطلان قول هؤلاء ، لأنك - ومن يستحق أن يخاطب - تعلمان أن هذه المحسوسات قد يقع عليها اسم واحد . لا على سبيل الاشتراك الصرف ، بل بحسب معنى واحد . مثل اسم الإنسان . فإنكما لا تشكان في أن وقوعه على زيد وعمرو ، بمعنى واحد : موجود . وذلك المعنى الموجود ، لا يخلو إما أن يكون بحيث يناله الحس، أو لا يكون . فإن كان بعيدا من أن يناله الحس، فقد أخرج التفتيش عن المحسوسات : ما ليس بمحسوس . وهذا أعجب. وإن كان محسوسا لأنه لا محالة وضع وأين ومقدار معين وكيف معين ، لا يتأتى أن يحس ، بل ولا أن يتخيل إلا كذلك . فإن كل محسوس وكل متخيل ، فإنه يتخصص لا محالة لشيء من هذه الأحوال . وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن ملائما لما ليس بتلك الحال ، فلم يكن مقولا على كثيرين مختلفين في تلك الحال فإذن الإنسان من حيث هو واحد الحقيقة بل من حيث حقيقته الأصلية التي لا تختلف فيها الكثرة غير محسوس ، بل معقول صرف. وكذلك الحال في كل كلي. ولعل قائلا منهم يقول : إن الإنسان مثلا إنما هو إنسان من حيث له أعضاء ، من يد وعين وحاجب وغير ذلك . ومن حيث هو كذلك ، فهو محسوس ، فتنبه ونقول له : إن الحال في كل عضو كلي ، مما ذكرته أو تركته . كالحال في الإنسان نفسه .
تنبيه : إنه لو كان كل موجود بحيث يدخل في الوهم والحس. لكان الحس والوهم يدخلان في الحس والوهم، ولكان العقل الذي هو الحكم الحق يدخل في الوهم . ومن أبعد هذه الأصول، فليس شيء من العشق والخجل والوجل والغضب والشجاعة والجبن، مما يدخل في الحس والوهم، وهي من علائق الأمور المحسوسة . فما ظنك بموجودات ، إن كانت خارجة الذوات عن درجات المحسوسات وعلائقها ، أهـ (2) فثبت : أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان ، مفهوم مجرد عن . فقد أخرج التفتيش عن المحسوس ما هو معقول مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المحسوسات ... الخ (س) والعبارة مصححة
The third proof: we know by necessity that the individuals of people share in the meaning understood from our saying 'man' and differ by their specific determinations and particularities, and what is shared is other than what makes them different. And this indicates that man insofar as he is man is an abstract concept free from specific form and specific place. So it is established that man is an intelligible, abstract concept. And if this is the case, then how could it be considered improbable by the intellect that the Creator of creatures is transcendent above sensory accidents and imaginative attachments? (1) Ibn Sina said in al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat, in the fourth mode of the metaphysics: Know that it often dominates the fancies of people that existent is the sensed, and that what the sense does not reach, the supposition of its existence is impossible. And that what does not become particularized by a place or a position by itself like a body, or by a cause in which it is like the states of a body, has no share of existence. And you, by reflection, can contemplate the sensed itself and learn from it the falsity of these people's statement, because you and whoever deserves to be addressed both know that these sensed things may be named by one name, not merely by equivocation, but according to one meaning, like the name 'man'. For both of you do not doubt that its application to Zayd and 'Amr in one meaning is existent. And that existent meaning is not free from either being such that the sense attains it, or not. If it is far from being attained by sense, then the investigation has extracted from the sensed what is not sensed, and this is more astonishing. And if it is sensed, then necessarily it has a position, a where, a specific magnitude, and a specific quality; it cannot be sensed, nor even imagined, except thus. For every sensed and every imagined thing necessarily becomes particularized by something of these states. And if it is so, it would not be compatible with what is not in that state, so it would not be predicated of many differing in that state. Thus man insofar as he is one in reality, but rather by his original reality which does not differ in multiplicity, is not sensed but is a pure intelligible. And the same holds for every universal. And perhaps one of them may say: man, for example, is only man insofar as he has limbs, like hand, eye, eyebrow, etc., and insofar as he is thus, he is sensed. So pay attention and say to him: the case for each limb is a universal, whether you mentioned it or left it, like the case for man himself. 
Warning: If every existent were such that it enters into imagination and sense, then sense and imagination would enter into sense and imagination, and the intellect, which is the true judge, would enter into imagination. And the most remote of these principles: there is nothing of love, shame, fear, anger, courage, and cowardice that enters into sense and imagination, and they are attachments of sensed things. So what do you think of existents whose essences are outside the degrees of sensed things and their attachments? End of quote. (2) So it is established that man insofar as he is man is an abstract concept from. Indeed, the investigation has extracted from the sensed what is an abstract intelligible. And if this is the case, then how could it be considered improbable by the intellect that the Creator of sensed things... etc. The phrase is corrected.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the transcendence of God from Al-Matalib al-'Aliyah. The proof is structured in two parts: the author's own argument followed by a supporting quotation from Ibn Sina, and then a reassertion of the conclusion. The author begins by stating a self-evident observation: all individual humans share a common meaning when we say 'man', yet they differ in their particular determinations. This shows that what makes them similar and what makes them different are distinct. From this it follows that 'man' considered purely as such is an abstract concept, free from any specific shape or spatial location. Therefore, 'man' is an intelligible, abstract concept. If this is accepted, then why would reason deem it improbable that the Creator of all beings – including these abstract concepts – is utterly transcendent beyond sensory accidents (lawāḥiq al-ḥiss) and imaginative attachments (ʿalāʾiq al-khayāl)? The author then quotes Ibn Sina to reinforce the argument. Ibn Sina argues that people mistakenly think only sensible things exist, but the fact that a single meaning like 'man' applies to many distinct individuals proves that there is a meaning that is not purely sensible. He shows that if the common meaning were sensible, it would have to be particularized with position and magnitude, making it impossible for it to be predicated of many diverse individuals. Therefore, the common meaning is a pure intelligible (maʿqūl ṣirf), not a sensible. Ibn Sina extends the argument: even mental states like love and fear are not sensible, so how much more so must there be existents entirely outside the realm of sense? The author then restates the conclusion: since the investigation has extracted abstract intelligibles from sensed things, it is not improbable that the Creator of those sensed things is Himself abstract and transcendent. The commentary emphasizes that the proof does not claim that the abstract universal exists in the external world as a separate entity, but rather that the concept itself has a reality that is not dependent on sensory particularization, and this opens the door to affirming a transcendent Creator.

Key Terms
الحس
الوهم
معقول
كلي
مفهوم مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Ibn Sina's argument implies that universals exist as separate Platonic forms.
Correction: Ibn Sina does not claim that the universal 'man' exists independently in the external world; he only argues that the common meaning is not a sensible particular, which is a logical point about predication, not a metaphysical claim about separate existence.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why can't the common meaning of 'man' be a sensible thing?
A) Because it is a mental construct with no relation to reality.
B) Because it would then have to be particularized in place and quality, making it impossible to be common.
C) Because senses cannot perceive abstract concepts.
D) Because all men are different.

Key Takeaway
Universal meanings are not sensible, so the Creator, who is beyond sense, is rationally conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you interact with people today, do you primarily see their physical appearance and circumstances, or do you recognize the shared humanity that points to a non-sensory reality? How will you train yourself to look beyond the sensory to the intelligible in your daily encounters?
?
Ibn Sina uses emotions like love and fear as examples of non-sensory yet real existents. Think of a time when your love for someone felt undeniable but could not be measured or touched. How does that experience challenge any tendency you have to reduce existence to what is material?
?
If your own essence as a human is not reducible to your body, what does that mean for how you prepare to meet your Creator? In what specific area of your life have you been living as if only the physical world matters, and what will you change starting today?

The Fifth Proof: Refuting the Karramiyya
Author refutes Karramiyya using dilemma of sensory pointability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the logical consequences of sensory pointability to refute the Karramiyya's position on divine corporeality.

Core Idea
If God were sensorily pointable, He would be either divisible or a point—both positions the Karramiyya reject.

The author continues his proofs for divine transcendence by addressing two opposing sects. In this first part, he targets the Karramiyya, who affirm that God is sensorily pointable yet deny that He is composite or a point. The author shows that their position is logically inconsistent, thereby using their own admissions against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dilemma structure: either divisible or indivisible.
2
Identify the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities for a sensorily pointable God: divisible (composite) or indivisible (like a point). Identify how each leads to a consequence the Karramiyya reject.
3
Examine the Karramiyya's response
The Karramiyya claim God is one, transcendent, yet not small. Note the author's counter: this is rejected by sense, imagination, and intellect.
The author argues that any pointable object must have extension or be a point—no middle ground.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the Karramiyya are obstinate: they reject a proof that is intuitive, and accept what is not intuitive. Reflect on why this method is effective.
The refutation is based on the opponents' own admissions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : إن خصومنا في هذا الباب إما الكرامية وإما الحنابلة . أما الكرامية. فإنا إذا قلنا لهم : لو كان الله تعالى مشارا إليه بالحس لكان ذلك الشيء إما أن يكون منقسما فيكون مركبا مؤلفا من الأبعاض والأجزاء [ وأنتم لا تقولون (٢) ] به وإما أن يكون غير منقسم فيكون في الصغر والحقارة مثل النقطة التي لا تنقسم ومثل الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ . وأنتم لا تقولون به . وعند هذا الكلام قالت الكرامية : إنه واحد منزه عن التركيب والتبعيض . ومع ذلك فإنه ليس بصغير ولا بحقير. ومعلوم أن هذا الذي ذكروه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال البتة ، بل لا يقبله العقل البتة . لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس . إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحياز كان أحد جانبيه مغايراً للثاني ، وذلك يوجب الانقسام، وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت ولا في القدام ولا في الخلف ، كان نقطة غير منقسمة ، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة . وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه تعالى غير قابل للقسمة مع القول بكونه عظيما غير متناه فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه تعالى ليس بمتحيز [ ولا حـال في المتحيز (١) ] مدفوع في بديهة العقل . فيثبت : أن الذاهب إلى هذا القول : متعنت . يحكم على ما كان بديهي البطلان بالصحة ، ويحكم على غير البديهي بكونه بديهيا . وأما الحنابلة الذين التزموا الأجزاء والأبعاض . فهم أيضا معترفون بأن ذات الله تعالى مخالفة لذوات هذه المحسوسات . فإنه تعالى لا يساوي هذه الذوات في قبول الاجتماع والافتراق ، وفي الصحة والمرض وفي الحياة والموت والصلابة واللين والاستئناس بالغير، والتوحش بسبب الوحدة . فهم معترفون بأن هذه الأشياء ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الله تعالى. فإنا إذا قلنا لهم : لو وصل شيء إلى ذاته. فهل يمكنه أن يغوص فيه أولا يمكنه ؟ فإن أمكنه كانت ذاته بمنزلة الماء والهواء. فتتمزق وتتفرق تلك الذات وإن تعذر عليه ذلك كانت ذاته بمنزلة الحجر الصلد . وأيضا : الحي الذي لا يأكل ولا يشرب ولا يطيب قلبه بسبب الاستئناس بالغير ، ولا تستوحش نفسه بسبب التفرد : غير معقول . فإن قيل للحنابلة : هذه الكلمات . قالوا : إنه تعالى يخالف خلقه . فلا يجوز أن يقاس حاله بحال غيره . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنهم قد اعترفوا في هذا المقام : بأن حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول في حق الله تعالى . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فكيف يمكنهم أن يحكموا بأن حكم الوهم والخيال في أن كل موجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً ، وإما أن يكون حالا في المتحيز : يجب أن يكون مقبولا ؟
The fifth proof: We say: Our opponents in this matter are either the Karramiyya or the Hanabila. As for the Karramiyya, if we say to them: If God were sensorily pointable, then that thing would either be divisible, thus composite and composed of parts and limbs—and you do not affirm that—or it would be indivisible, thus in smallness and contempt like a point that does not divide and like a part that cannot be further divided—and you do not affirm that. At this statement the Karramiyya said: He is one, transcendent above composition and partition, yet He is not small nor contemptible. It is known that what they have mentioned is not accepted by sense or imagination at all, nor even by the intellect at all. Because that which is pointed to by sense: if it has extension in directions and spaces, then one of its sides is different from the other, and that necessitates division; if it has no extension in any direction—neither right nor left, above nor below, front nor back—then it is an indivisible point, and is extremely small and contemptible. And if they do not find it far-fetched to affirm that He is not divisible while claiming that He is immensely great without limit, then how can they judge that the statement that He is not a spatial atom (mutahayyiz) nor inhering in a spatial atom is rejected by primordial reason? So it is established that the one who holds this view is obstinate: he judges what is intuitively false to be true, and judges what is not intuitive to be intuitive. As for the Hanabila who affirm parts and limbs, they also admit that God's essence is unlike the essences of these sensible things. Indeed, He is not equal to these essences in accepting conjunction and separation, health and illness, life and death, hardness and softness, familiarity with others, and loneliness due to solitude. They admit that these things are impossible with respect to God. For if we say to them: If something were to reach His essence, could it penetrate it or not? If it could, then His essence would be like water and air, thus that essence would be torn and separated. If it could not, then His essence would be like hard stone. Also: a living being that does not eat or drink, whose heart is not pleased by companionship, and whose soul is not distressed by solitude—is inconceivable. If it is said to the Hanabila these statements, they say: He is unlike His creation, so His state cannot be measured by the state of others. And if this is established, we say: They have admitted in this context that the judgment of imagination and illusion is not acceptable concerning God. And if that is so, how can they judge that the judgment of imagination and illusion that every existent must be either a spatial atom (mutahayyiz) or inhering in a spatial atom must be accepted?
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisa), which is a refutation of two opposing sects: the Karramiyya and the Hanabila. The author first addresses the Karramiyya. He argues that if God were perceivable by the senses (mushāran ilayhi bi-l-ḥiss), He would have to be either divisible (munqasim) or indivisible. If divisible, He would be composite—which the Karramiyya deny. If indivisible, He would be like a point—which they also deny. The Karramiyya respond by saying God is one, transcendent above composition and partition, yet not small or contemptible. The author counters that this claim is not accepted by sense, imagination, or intellect. A sensorily pointable object must have extension, leading to division, or be a point, which is tiny. Since the Karramiyya claim God is great and indivisible, they contradict themselves. Therefore, they are being obstinate (mutaʿannit) by rejecting a proof they already implicitly accept. Then the author turns to the Hanabila, who affirm parts and limbs for God. He shows that they admit God is unlike created bodies in many respects (e.g., conjunction, separation, health, illness). Moreover, they would agree that if something reached God's essence, it would either penetrate (making God like water) or not (making God like stone)—both unacceptable. They also admit that a living being that does not eat, drink, or interact is inconceivable. When confronted, the Hanabila respond that God is unlike creation and cannot be measured by analogy. The author uses this concession against them: if the judgment of imagination and illusion (al-wahm wa-l-khayāl) is not applicable to God, then the Hanabila cannot rely on that same judgment to claim that every existent must be either a spatial atom (mutahayyiz) or inhering in a spatial atom (ḥall fī l-mutaḥayyiz). Thus, the fifth proof shows that both sects, through their own admissions, undermine their objection to abstract divine existence.

الكرامية
مشار إليه بالحس
منقسم
مركب
نقطة

Misconception: The author simply dismisses the Karramiyya position without argument.
Correction: The author actually uses a logical dilemma based on the Karramiyya's own admissions to demonstrate inconsistency.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which two possibilities arise if God is sensorily pointable?
A) He is either eternal or temporal.
B) He is either divisible or indivisible.
C) He is either knowing or ignorant.
D) He is either powerful or weak.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the Karramiyya by showing that their belief in a sensorily pointable God leads to a logical contradiction they cannot resolve.

The Fifth Proof: Refuting the Hanabila
Author refutes Hanabila using their own admission of divine dissimilarity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the Hanabila's own admission of divine dissimilarity to refute their reliance on imagination in theology.

Core Idea
If the Hanabila admit God is unlike creation, they cannot then use imagination to argue He must be a spatial atom.

Having refuted the Karramiyya, the author now turns to the Hanabila, who affirm divine parts and limbs. He shows that they too admit God is unlike created bodies in fundamental ways. From this concession, he argues that they cannot consistently rely on the judgment of imagination and illusion to deny that God exists without spatial location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the shift from the Karramiyya to the Hanabila.
The Hanabila admit divine dissimilarity (mukhālafa) to creation.
2
Identify the Hanabila's admissions
The author lists several aspects in which the Hanabila admit God is unlike created bodies: conjunction/separation, health/illness, etc. Mark these in the text.
Also note the thought experiment about penetration.
3
Understand the Hanabila's response
When pressed with analogies, the Hanabila respond: 'He is unlike His creation.' See how the author uses this response as a concession.
4
Grasp the final argument
The author concludes: since the Hanabila reject the judgment of imagination concerning God, they cannot rely on that same judgment to assert that every existent must be either a spatial atom or inhering in one.
This undermines their objection to abstract divine existence.

The fifth proof: We say: Our opponents in this matter are either the Karramiyya or the Hanabila. As for the Karramiyya, if we say to them: If God were sensorily pointable, then that thing would either be divisible, thus composite and composed of parts and limbs—and you do not affirm that—or it would be indivisible, thus in smallness and contempt like a point that does not divide and like a part that cannot be further divided—and you do not affirm that. It is known that what they have mentioned is not accepted by sense or imagination at all, nor even by the intellect at all. As for the Hanabila who affirm parts and limbs, they also admit that God's essence is unlike the essences of these sensible things. Indeed, He is not equal to these essences in accepting conjunction and separation, health and illness, life and death, hardness and softness, familiarity with others, and loneliness due to solitude. They admit that these things are impossible with respect to God. For if we say to them: If something were to reach His essence, could it penetrate it or not? If it could, then His essence would be like water and air, thus that essence would be torn and separated. If it could not, then His essence would be like hard stone. Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-ḥujja al-khāmisa), which is a refutation of two opposing sects: the Karramiyya and the Hanabila. The author first addresses the Karramiyya. He argues that if God were perceivable by the senses (mushāran ilayhi bi-l-ḥiss), He would have to be either divisible (munqasim) or indivisible. If divisible, He would be composite—which the Karramiyya deny. If indivisible, He would be like a point—which they also deny. The Karramiyya respond by saying God is one, transcendent above composition and partition, yet not small or contemptible. The author counters that this claim is not accepted by sense, imagination, or intellect. A sensorily pointable object must have extension, leading to division, or be a point, which is tiny. Since the Karramiyya claim God is great and indivisible, they contradict themselves. Therefore, they are being obstinate (mutaʿannit) by rejecting a proof they already implicitly accept. Then the author turns to the Hanabila, who affirm parts and limbs for God. He shows that they admit God is unlike created bodies in many respects (e.g., conjunction, separation, health, illness). Moreover, they would agree that if something reached God's essence, it would either penetrate (making God like water) or not (making God like stone)—both unacceptable. They also admit that a living being that does not eat, drink, or interact is inconceivable. When confronted, the Hanabila respond that God is unlike creation and cannot be measured by analogy. The author uses this concession against them: if the judgment of imagination and illusion (al-wahm wa-l-khayāl) is not applicable to God, then the Hanabila cannot rely on that same judgment to claim that every existent must be either a spatial atom (mutahayyiz) or inhering in a spatial atom (ḥall fī l-mutaḥayyiz). Thus, the fifth proof shows that both sects, through their own admissions, undermine their objection to abstract divine existence.

According to the author, what do the Hanabila admit about God's essence?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining God as occupying a place like a throne or a direction, even though you intellectually know He is unlike anything you can picture? How will you actively train your mind to reject such imaginative assumptions when you think of Him?
?
If the Hanabila, who believe God has parts, still admit that imagination fails when applied to God, what does that say about your own willingness to let go of comfortable mental images of God? Are you ready to accept that God's reality transcends every picture your mind can form, even if it feels unsettling?
?
The Hanabila's inconsistency lies in using imagination selectively—sometimes invoking mukhālafa, sometimes ignoring it. Where in your own beliefs about God might you be doing the same: accepting a scriptural description at face value but then using reason to impose categories God transcends? Repent from that inconsistency now.

Divine Transcendence

Proof for Space and Time as Non-Corporeal Existents
Razi proves the existence of void and time as non-corporeal existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi argues that space (void) and time are real non-corporeal entities, distinct from bodies and accidents.

Core Idea
Space and time are neither bodies nor accidents—they are real abstract existents.

This passage from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's al-Matalib al-Aliya presents a rational proof for the existence of space and time as non-corporeal realities. Razi first establishes that space (al-fada') and void (al-khala') exist independently of bodies. He then argues that time, being divisible and comparable in magnitude, cannot be mere non-existence and must be an existent that is neither a body nor an accident. This argument is crucial for Islamic theology as it opens the door to affirming the existence of non-corporeal beings, including the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the English translation. Notice how Razi builds his argument in two parts: first for space, then for time.
The passage uses a trilemma for time: it must be a body, accident, or neither.
2
Identify the proof for space
Razi states that space and void exist, and they cannot be another place (infinite regress). What is the key conclusion about space?
The conclusion is that space is an existent not located in place or volume.
3
Examine the proof for time
Razi uses divisibility into parts (years, months, days, hours) to argue that time must be existent, since pure non-existence cannot be divided. Then he eliminates the possibilities that time is a body or an accident.
Pay attention to the argument against time being a body: proximity to the body would correlate with proximity to time.
4
Understand the theological implications
Razi concludes that affirming non-corporeal existents like space and time is necessary, and this paves the way for understanding God's transcendence.
This proof is considered the strongest in the chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن الفضاء والخلاء أمر موجود. وظاهر أنه ليس مكانا آخر ، وإلا لزم التسلسل . فقد حصل في الوجود موجود ليس حاصلا في المكان والحيز . وأما الزمان فهو أيضا موجود. والدليل عليه : هو أنا نقسمه إلى السنين ، ونقسم السنين إلى الشهور ، ونقسم الشهور إلى الأيام. ونقسم اليوم إلى الساعات . ونحكم بأن الساعة الواحدة أقل قدرا من اليوم، الذي هو أقل من الشهر . الذي هو أقل من السنة . والعدم المحض والنفي الصرف ، يمتنع كونه قابلا للتقسيم إلى الأجزاء ، ويمتنع وصفه بكونه أقل من الأجزاء أو أكثر منها . فيثبت أنه شيء موجود . وذلك الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً أو حالا في المتحيز ، أولا متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز ويمتنع كونه متحيزا وإلا لكان شيء يكون أقرب إلى ذلك المتحيز ، كان أقرب إلى الزمان ، وكل ما كان أبعد عن ذلك المتحيز ، كان أبعد من الزمان . ومعلوم بالضرورة : أن ذلك باطل قطعا . لأن الأشياء الموجودة في هذا اليوم أكمل من بعض ، والعلم به ضروري. وبهذا الطريق ثبت أيضا : أنه يمتنع عرضا حالا في المتحيز ، وإذا بطل هذان القمان ، ثبت أن الزمان والمدة موجود من الموجودات مع أنه غير متحيز ولا حال في المتحيز . وذلك هو المطلوب . واعلم : أنك إذا تأملت ما لخصناه في مسألة الخلاء والمدة في هذا الكتاب ، ووقفت على مذاهب الناس فيهما ، ووقفت على أن الحق هو القول بإثبات الخلاء ، وعلى أن الحق هو أن المدة ليس بمتحيز ولا حركة ، ولكنه موجود مجرد . عرفت قطعا أن القول بإثبات موجود مجرد عن الجسمية : أمر واجب الاعتراف به . وهذا الوجه أقوى الوجوه المذكورة في هذا الباب
That space and void are an existent thing. And it is apparent that it is not another place, otherwise there would be infinite regress. So there has come into existence an existent that is not located in place and volume. As for time, it too is existent. The proof of it is that we divide it into years, and we divide years into months, and we divide months into days, and we divide the day into hours, and we judge that one hour is smaller in measure than a day, which is smaller than a month, which is smaller than a year. Pure non-existence and absolute negation cannot be subject to division into parts, nor can it be described as being smaller or larger than parts. So it is established that it is an existent thing. That existent is either a body or an accident in a body, or neither a body nor an accident in a body. It is impossible for it to be a body, because then something closer to that body would be closer to time, and something farther from that body would be farther from time. And it is known by necessity that this is absolutely false. For the things existent in this day are more complete than one another, and knowledge of it is necessary. By this method it is also established that it cannot be an accident in a body. And when these two possibilities are invalidated, it is established that time and duration are existent among existents, while being neither a body nor an accident in a body. That is what was desired. And know: if you ponder what we have summarized in this book on the issue of void and duration, and you come to know the doctrines of people concerning them, and you come to know that the truth is the affirmation of void, and that the truth is that duration is not a body nor motion, but it is an abstract existent, you will know categorically that affirming the existence of an existent abstracted from corporeality is a matter that must be acknowledged. And this proof is the strongest of the proofs mentioned in this chapter.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the existence of space (al-fada') and void (al-khala') and time (al-zaman) as non-corporeal entities. The author begins by stating that space and void exist and are clearly not another place, otherwise infinite regress would follow. Thus, there exists something that is not located in place or volume. Then he turns to time, providing a proof: time is divisible into years, months, days, hours, and we judge that some parts are smaller than others. Pure non-existence cannot be divided or have parts of different sizes, so time must be something existent. That existent must be either a body, an accident in a body, or neither. It cannot be a body because if it were a body, then proximity to that body would correlate with proximity to time, but that is absurd. Nor can it be an accident in a body. Therefore, time and duration exist while being neither body nor accident. This argument is then tied to the existence of void and duration as abstract entities, and the author concludes that this is the strongest proof for affirming a non-corporeal existent, which is crucial for establishing God's transcendence.

حال
عدم محض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is merely a subjective measure.
Correction: Razi argues that time has objective existence as a non-corporeal entity, as shown by its divisibility and comparability, which cannot be true of pure non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that time cannot be a body?
A) Because bodies cannot be divided.
B) Because if time were a body, proximity to that body would correlate with proximity to time, which is false.
C) Because time is not measurable.
D) Because bodies are always accidents.

Key Takeaway
Space and time are real, non-corporeal existents, proving that not all realities are material.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time is a real non-corporeal existent, not just a human construct, then every moment you live has objective reality before the Creator who is beyond time. Are you living each moment as if it will be accounted for, or as if it slips into nothingness? What would change if you truly believed that time is a created entity that will be questioned?
?
Razi proved that not all realities are physical. What is one area in your life where you are acting as if only the material world matters—like pursuing money, comfort, or status as if they are ultimate? How can you start treating the unseen realities (God, angels, afterlife) as more real than what you can touch?
?
You are convinced that God exists without being in a place. Yet do you act as if He is present with you right now, in this room, in this moment? What would you do differently in the next hour if you were fully aware that the non-corporeal Creator is witnessing you? Be specific.

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
8 min
Mutual Refutation of Corporealist and Temporalist Views
Razi presents a mutual refutation of corporealist and temporalist views about God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes both corporealist and temporalist views by showing they rely on imagination and that God's transcendence is beyond direction and time.

Core Idea
Each side admits the other's argument is fancy — the truth is beyond direction and time.

Razi presents a clever mutual refutation: two opposing sects each rely on assumptions the other shows are imaginary. By letting them cancel each other out, he clears the way for a higher understanding of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two positions
The corporealist says any two existents must interpenetrate or be separated by a direction. The temporalist says they must coexist or one precede the other with or without a duration.
Both positions assume a necessary connection between existents.
2
Apply to God and the world
Razi applies each position to the Creator and the world, showing that each leads to absurd consequences (e.g., God being in a direction or requiring pre-existing time).
This exposes the inadequacy of each framework.
3
Note the mutual concession
Each side admits the other's view is based on illusion (wahm) and not reality. This mutual admission invalidates both positions.
The refutation is implicit in the conflict itself.
4
Grasp the positive conclusion
The truth is that God is separate from the world not by direction, and prior not by time. Direction and time themselves are not located in anything else.
This establishes God's absolute transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن المجسم يقول : كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، سريان العرض في الجسم، أو يكون مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات. فأما إثبات موجودين لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، ولا يكون مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات فهذا مما لا يقبله العقل . وأما الدهري . فإنه يقول : كل موجودين ، فلا بد وأن يوجدا معا ، أو أن يوجد أحدهما قبل الآخر. والذي يكون قبل الآخر ، إما أن لا توجد بينهما فاصلة بمدة متناهية ، أو فاصلة بمدة غير متناهية . إذا ثبت هذا التقسيم ، فنقول : الباري والعالم. إن وجدا معا ، لزم أن يكونا قديمين معا ، أو محدثين معا . فأما القول بقدم الله تعالى وحدوث العالم على هذا التقدير : فهو غير مقبول ، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا ، مع أن الله تقدمه من غير فاصلة أو تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية . فهذا أيضا يقتضي حدوث ذات الله تعالى ، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا ، مع أن الله تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية . فهذا يقتضي قدم المدة والزمان . وأما فرض كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم ، لا على أحد هذه الأقسام ، فذاك مما لا يقبله العقل . فالحاصل : أن المجسم لا يمكنه أن يعقل كونه تعالى مباينا للعالم إلا بالحيز والجهة . والدهري لا يعقل كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم إلا بالمدة والزمان . ثم إن المجسم مقر بأن الذي يقوله الدهري من عمل الوهم والخيال . وأنه في نفسه ليس بحق . والدهري معترف بأن الذي يقوله المجسم من عمل الوهم والخيال . وأنه في نفسه كاذب . فصار قول كل واحد منها معارضا بقول الآخر ، ويظهر منه بطلان كلا القولين وأن الحق : أنه تعالى مباين للعالم لا بسبب الحيز والجهة . وأنه تعالى سابق على العالم لا بسبب المدة والزمان . بل نقول : إن الجهة المعينة مباينة لسائر الجهات لا بسبب الحيز والجهة ، والزمان المعين مباين لسائر الأزمنة لا بسبب المدة والزمان . فإن الزمان غير حاصل في زمان آخر. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك في الجهة وفي المدة . فبأن
The tenth proof: The corporealist (al-mujassim) says: For every two existents, one must be permeating in the other, like the permeation of an accident in a body, or be separate from it by a direction among directions. As for affirming two existents where one is not permeating in the other, nor separate from it by any direction, this is something the intellect does not accept. As for the temporalist (al-dahri), he says: For every two existents, they must exist together, or one must exist before the other. And that which is before the other is either with no finite duration separating them, or with an infinite duration separating them. Once this division is established, we say: The Creator and the world. If they exist together, it follows that both are eternal or both are originated. As for the statement that God is eternal and the world originated on this assumption, it is unacceptable. As for the statement that the world is originated while God preceded it without a separating duration, or preceded it by an infinite duration, this also entails the origination of God's essence. As for the statement that the world is originated while God preceded it by an infinite duration, this entails the eternity of duration and time. As for supposing that He, exalted, is prior to the world without being in any of these categories, this is something the intellect does not accept. The conclusion: The corporealist cannot conceive of God being separate from the world except by volume and direction. The temporalist cannot conceive of God being prior to the world except by duration and time. Then the corporealist admits that what the temporalist says is the work of illusion and imagination, and that in itself it is not true. And the temporalist admits that what the corporealist says is the work of illusion and imagination, and that in itself it is false. So the statement of each refutes the other, and it appears from this the falsity of both statements, and that the truth is that He, exalted, is separate from the world not because of volume and direction, and that He, exalted, is prior to the world not because of duration and time. Rather we say: A specific direction is distinct from other directions not because of volume and direction, and a specific time is distinct from other times not because of duration and time. For time does not exist in another time. And if this is the case concerning direction and duration, then by...
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument (al-hujja al-ashira) against corporealist (mujassim) and temporalist (dahri) conceptions of God's relationship to the world. The author first states the corporealist position: any two existents must either interpenetrate or be separated by a direction; they cannot exist without being in some relation of space. The temporalist position: any two existents must exist together or one before the other, with a finite or infinite interval. Applying these to God and the world, the author shows that the corporealist view leads to absurdities (e.g., God must be in a direction) and the temporalist view leads to absurdities (e.g., requiring pre-existing time or the origination of God). Each side implicitly admits that the other's position is based on imagination (wahm) and not reality. Therefore, both are false, and the truth is that God is separate from the world without spatial location and prior to the world without temporal precedence. The author adds that direction and time themselves are distinct from other directions and times not through physical or temporal means, which suggests a metaphysical transcendence. The argument is incomplete at the end of the page, but the core mutual refutation is clear.

Key Terms

الدهري
مباينة
جهة
مدة
زمان
وهم
خيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's transcendence means He is separated from the world in the same way as physical things are separated by direction or time.
Correction: Razi argues that true transcendence is beyond direction and time; direction and time themselves are not located in anything else.

Checkpoint
How do the corporealist and temporalist views refute each other in this passage?
A) The corporealist proves the existence of direction, while the temporalist proves the existence of time.
B) Each side admits that the other's view is based on illusion and imagination, showing both to be false.
C) They both agree that God is prior to the world.
D) The corporealist argues for interpenetration, but the temporalist argues for separation.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that both corporealist and temporalist views of God's relation to the world are based on illusion, and that true transcendence is beyond direction and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that both the corporealist and temporalist admit each other's view is mere imagination. Do you still have a mental image of God as somehow 'outside' the universe or 'before' time? Will you repent from these images tonight in your prayer and submit to the God who is beyond all categories?
?
If your intellect cannot accept a being without direction or time, as Razi notes, but revelation demands it, are you willing to let go of your intellectual pride and believe? What specific image of God will you struggle to abandon?
?
Razi concludes that direction and time themselves are not located in anything else. This means God is not in a place or time. When you call upon Him in your du'a, do you imagine Him somewhere listening? Can you call upon Him without any image, trusting that He hears though He is beyond space?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

Refuting the Materialist and Anthropomorphist Views on God's Relation to the World
The author refutes both مجسم and دهري views on God's relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes both the anthropomorphist and materialist conceptions of God's relationship to the world, showing their internal inconsistencies.

Core Idea
Both spatial separation and temporal priority fail to describe God's transcendence.

The author tackles two opposing heresies: the anthropomorphist who insists God must be in a direction, and the materialist who insists God must be prior in time. By examining their arguments, he shows that each view leads to absurdities when applied to God. This lesson walks through those refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for Lesson 1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author sets up the problem: the intellect rejects certain conceptions.
The passage starts with 'من الجهات' and ends with 'بطلان كلا القولين'.
2
Understand the materialist's division
The materialist argues that any two existents either coexist or one precedes the other, with finite or infinite interval. Apply this to God and world.
The author lists four possibilities and shows each fails.
3
See the mutual refutation
Each sect admits the other's view is based on imagination, so both are false. The author uses this to show the need for a different conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الجهات. فأما إثبات موجودين لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر، ولا يكون مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات فهذا مما لا يقبله العقل.
وأما الدهري فإنه يقول: كل موجودين، فلا بد وأن يوجدا معا، أو أن يوجد أحدهما قبل الآخر. والذي يكون قبل الآخر، إما أن لا توجد بينهما فاصلة بمدة متناهية، أو فاصلة بمدة غير متناهية. إذا ثبت هذا التقسيم، فنقول: الباري والعالم. إن وجدا معا، لزم أن يكونا قديمين معا، أو محدثين معا. فأما القول بقدم الله تعالى وحدوث العالم على هذا التقدير: فهو غير مقبول، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا، مع أن الله تقدمه من غير فاصلة أو تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية. فهذا أيضا يقتضي حدوث ذات الله تعالى، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا، مع أن الله تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية. فهذا يقتضي قدم المدة والزمان. وأما فرض كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم، لا على أحد هذه الأقسام، فذاك مما لا يقبله العقل.
فالحاصل: أن المجسم لا يمكنه أن يعقل كونه تعالى مباينا للعالم إلا بالحيز والجهة. والدهري لا يعقل كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم إلا بالمدة والزمان. ثم إن المجسم مقر بأن الذي يقوله الدهري من عمل الوهم والخيال، وأنه في نفسه ليس بحق. والدهري معترف بأن الذي يقوله المجسم من عمل الوهم والخيال، وأنه في نفسه كاذب. فصار قول كل واحد منها معارضا بقول الآخر، ويظهر منه بطلان كلا القولين وأن الحق: أنه تعالى مباين للعالم لا بسبب الحيز والجهة. وأنه تعالى سابق على العالم لا بسبب والزمان. بل نقول: إن الجهة المعينة مباينة لسائر الجهات لا بسبب الحيز والجهة، والزمان المعين مباين لسائر الأزمنة لا بسبب المدة والزمان. فإن الزمان غير حاصل في زمان آخر. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك في الجهة وفي المدة، فبأن يكون خالق الجهة والمدة منزها عن المباينة بالمكان والجهة، ومنزها عن السبق على العالم بالزمان والمدة كان أولى.
ولهذا قال الأمام الأجل علي بن أبي طالب عليه السلام: «الذي أين الأين لا يقال له: أين؟ والذي كيف الكيف لا يقال له: كيف؟»، ونقل عن الفيلسوف أرسطاطاليس أنه كتب في أول كتابه في الإلهيات: «من أراد أن يشرع في المعارف الإلهية، فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى»، ومراده: أن الإنسان ألف أحكام الوهم والخيال، والمباحث الإلهية لا يوافقها أحكام الأوهام والخيالات. فلهذا السبب وجب على هذا الطالب استحداث فطرة أخرى.
فهذا جملة الكلام في بيان أن ادعاء البديهة في نفي موجود، لا يكون موجودا داخل العالم ولا خارجه: قول باطل.
From directions. As for affirming two existents such that one is not penetrating the other, nor is it separate from it by a direction from the directions, then this is what the intellect does not accept. As for the materialist, he says: any two existents must necessarily either exist together, or one of them exists before the other. And that which is before the other is either that there is no interval between them of a finite duration, or an interval of an infinite duration. If this division is established, we say: the Creator and the world. If they existed together, it would necessitate that both are eternal or both are originated. As for the statement that God is eternal and the world originated on this assumption, it is unacceptable. And as for the statement that the world is originated while God precedes it without an interval or precedes it by an infinite duration, this also necessitates the origination of the essence of God. And as for the statement that the world is originated while God precedes it by an infinite duration, this necessitates the eternity of duration and time. And as for the supposition that He, exalted, precedes the world in none of these divisions, that is what the intellect does not accept. The result: the anthropomorphist cannot conceive of God being separate from the world except by space and direction. And the materialist cannot conceive of God preceding the world except by duration and time. Then the anthropomorphist admits that what the materialist says is a product of imagination and fantasy, and that it is not true in itself. And the materialist acknowledges that what the anthropomorphist says is a product of imagination and fantasy, and that it is false in itself. So the statement of each of them is opposed by the statement of the other, and from this the falsity of both statements becomes apparent, and that the truth is that He, exalted, is separate from the world not by space and direction, and that He, exalted, precedes the world not by time and duration. Rather we say: the specific direction is separate from other directions not by space and direction, and the specific time is separate from other times not by duration and time. For time does not subsist in another time. And if the matter is thus concerning direction and duration, then it is more fitting that the Creator of direction and duration be exalted above separation by place and direction, and exalted above priority over the world by time and duration. For this reason, the supreme Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib, peace be upon him, said: "He who is the 'where' of where, it is not said to Him: where? And He who is the 'how' of how, it is not said to Him: how?" And it is narrated from the philosopher Aristotle that he wrote at the beginning of his book on metaphysics: "Whoever wants to embark on divine sciences should acquire for himself another nature." His meaning is that a person is accustomed to the judgments of imagination and fantasy, and the divine investigations are not compatible with the judgments of imaginations and fantasies. For this reason, this seeker must acquire another nature. This is the sum of the discourse in clarifying that the claim of intuition in denying an existent that is neither inside the world nor outside it is a false statement.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two opposing views on God's relationship to the world: the anthropomorphist (مجسم) who insists on spatial separation, and the materialist (دهري) who insists on temporal priority. The author begins by stating that the intellect rejects the notion of two existents that are neither interpenetrating nor separate in a direction. He then examines the materialist's division: either two existents coexist or one precedes the other, and the interval is finite or infinite. Applying this to God and the world, he shows all possibilities lead to absurdity: coexistence implies both eternal or both originated; temporal priority with no interval or an infinite interval implies origination of God or eternity of time. Thus, the intellect rejects all such schemes. The author then concludes that the anthropomorphist can only conceive separation by space, and the materialist only priority by time. Each admits the other's view is imaginary, so both are refuted. The truth is that God is separate from the world not by space/direction, and prior to it not by time/duration. He clarifies that even specific directions and times are distinct without needing further dimensions. Hence, the Creator of direction and time is even more exalted. The author supports this with a saying of Ali ibn Abi Talib and a quote from Aristotle, emphasizing that divine matters require a different faculty beyond imagination. The passage ends by declaring that the claim of intuitive knowledge (بديهة) denying an existent neither inside nor outside the world is false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God being separate from the world implies a spatial direction or location.
Correction: The author argues that God's separation is beyond space; the anthropomorphist's view is refuted.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the materialist's division of temporal priority as applied to God and the world?
A) Because it leads to the eternity of time, which is impossible.
B) Because God cannot be prior in time, only in essence.
C) Because the division includes options that imply God's origination or eternity of time, both of which are false.

Key Takeaway
Both the anthropomorphist and materialist conceptions of God's relation to the world are based on imagination and lead to absurdities; true transcendence is beyond space and time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Affirming Divine Transcendence Beyond Space and Time
The author affirms divine transcendence and cites authoritative sayings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the positive doctrine of God's transcendence, supported by rational argument and authoritative quotes, and the need for a new intellectual faculty.

Core Idea
God is separate from the world not by space, and prior to it not by time.

Having refuted the flawed conceptions, the author now presents the correct understanding: God's transcendence is absolute, not limited by space or time. He reinforces this with a saying of Imam Ali and a quote from Aristotle, emphasizing that such truths require transcending ordinary imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the positive affirmation
Read the passage starting from 'وأن الحق' onwards. Notice the clear statement: God is separate not by space, prior not by time.
2
Understand the argument by analogy
The author shows that specific directions and times are distinct without needing further dimensions, so the Creator is even more exalted.
This is an a fortiori argument (اولى).
3
Engage with the quotes
Reflect on Imam Ali's words and Aristotle's advice. Why do they emphasize a new faculty?
The point is that imagination (وهم) cannot grasp divine realities.
4
Connect to the final conclusion
The passage ends by rejecting the claim that intuition (بديهة) can deny an existent neither inside nor outside the world.

من الجهات. فأما إثبات موجودين لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر، ولا يكون مباينا عنه بجهة من الجهات فهذا مما لا يقبله العقل.
وأما الدهري فإنه يقول: كل موجودين، فلا بد وأن يوجدا معا، أو أن يوجد أحدهما قبل الآخر. والذي يكون قبل الآخر، إما أن لا توجد بينهما فاصلة بمدة متناهية، أو فاصلة بمدة غير متناهية. إذا ثبت هذا التقسيم، فنقول: الباري والعالم. إن وجدا معا، لزم أن يكونا قديمين معا، أو محدثين معا. فأما القول بقدم الله تعالى وحدوث العالم على هذا التقدير: فهو غير مقبول، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا، مع أن الله تقدمه من غير فاصلة أو تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية. فهذا أيضا يقتضي حدوث ذات الله تعالى، وأما القول بكون العالم محدثا، مع أن الله تقدمه بمدة غير متناهية. فهذا يقتضي قدم المدة والزمان. وأما فرض كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم، لا على أحد هذه الأقسام، فذاك مما لا يقبله العقل.
فالحاصل: أن المجسم لا يمكنه أن يعقل كونه تعالى مباينا للعالم إلا بالحيز والجهة. والدهري لا يعقل كونه تعالى متقدما على العالم إلا بالمدة والزمان. ثم إن المجسم مقر بأن الذي يقوله الدهري من عمل الوهم والخيال، وأنه في نفسه ليس بحق. والدهري معترف بأن الذي يقوله المجسم من عمل الوهم والخيال، وأنه في نفسه كاذب. فصار قول كل واحد منها معارضا بقول الآخر، ويظهر منه بطلان كلا القولين وأن الحق: أنه تعالى مباين للعالم لا بسبب الحيز والجهة. وأنه تعالى سابق على العالم لا بسبب والزمان. بل نقول: إن الجهة المعينة مباينة لسائر الجهات لا بسبب الحيز والجهة، والزمان المعين مباين لسائر الأزمنة لا بسبب المدة والزمان. فإن الزمان غير حاصل في زمان آخر. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك في الجهة وفي المدة، فبأن يكون خالق الجهة والمدة منزها عن المباينة بالمكان والجهة، ومنزها عن السبق على العالم بالزمان والمدة كان أولى.
ولهذا قال الأمام الأجل علي بن أبي طالب عليه السلام: «الذي أين الأين لا يقال له: أين؟ والذي كيف الكيف لا يقال له: كيف؟»، ونقل عن الفيلسوف أرسطاطاليس أنه كتب في أول كتابه في الإلهيات: «من أراد أن يشرع في المعارف الإلهية، فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى»، ومراده: أن الإنسان ألف أحكام الوهم والخيال، والمباحث الإلهية لا يوافقها أحكام الأوهام والخيالات. فلهذا السبب وجب على هذا الطالب استحداث فطرة أخرى.
فهذا جملة الكلام في بيان أن ادعاء البديهة في نفي موجود، لا يكون موجودا داخل العالم ولا خارجه: قول باطل.
From directions. As for affirming two existents such that one is not penetrating the other, nor is it separate from it by a direction from the directions, then this is what the intellect does not accept. As for the materialist, he says: any two existents must necessarily either exist together, or one of them exists before the other. And that which is before the other is either that there is no interval between them of a finite duration, or an interval of an infinite duration. If this division is established, we say: the Creator and the world. If they existed together, it would necessitate that both are eternal or both are originated. As for the statement that God is eternal and the world originated on this assumption, it is unacceptable. And as for the statement that the world is originated while God precedes it without an interval or precedes it by an infinite duration, this also necessitates the origination of the essence of God. And as for the statement that the world is originated while God precedes it by an infinite duration, this necessitates the eternity of duration and time. And as for the supposition that He, exalted, precedes the world in none of these divisions, that is what the intellect does not accept. The result: the anthropomorphist cannot conceive of God being separate from the world except by space and direction. And the materialist cannot conceive of God preceding the world except by duration and time. Then the anthropomorphist admits that what the materialist says is a product of imagination and fantasy, and that it is not true in itself. And the materialist acknowledges that what the anthropomorphist says is a product of imagination and fantasy, and that it is false in itself. So the statement of each of them is opposed by the statement of the other, and from this the falsity of both statements becomes apparent, and that the truth is that He, exalted, is separate from the world not by space and direction, and that He, exalted, precedes the world not by time and duration. Rather we say: the specific direction is separate from other directions not by space and direction, and the specific time is separate from other times not by duration and time. For time does not subsist in another time. And if the matter is thus concerning direction and duration, then it is more fitting that the Creator of direction and duration be exalted above separation by place and direction, and exalted above priority over the world by time and duration. For this reason, the supreme Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib, peace be upon him, said: "He who is the 'where' of where, it is not said to Him: where? And He who is the 'how' of how, it is not said to Him: how?" And it is narrated from the philosopher Aristotle that he wrote at the beginning of his book on metaphysics: "Whoever wants to embark on divine sciences should acquire for himself another nature." His meaning is that a person is accustomed to the judgments of imagination and fantasy, and the divine investigations are not compatible with the judgments of imaginations and fantasies. For this reason, this seeker must acquire another nature. This is the sum of the discourse in clarifying that the claim of intuition in denying an existent that is neither inside the world nor outside it is a false statement.

He clarifies that even specific directions and times are distinct without needing further dimensions. Hence, the Creator of direction and time is even more exalted.
