God's Transcendence Beyond Place and Time
Argues for divine transcendence with refutation of a false claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Creator cannot be confined by space, time, or direction, and why ordinary imagination cannot judge divine realities.

Core Idea
The Creator of space and time is beyond both.

This passage tackles a profound theological claim: that an existent can be neither inside nor outside the world. Using quotes from Imam Ali and Aristotle, the author argues that such a claim is not self-evidently impossible, because divine realities transcend the categories of human imagination. Prepare to see why ordinary thinking falls short when addressing the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice the flow from statement to quotations to conclusion. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the key terms 'جهة', 'مدة', 'مباينة', 'بديهة'.
2
Identify the thesis
The opening sentence gives the main thesis: the Creator of space and duration is transcendent of place, direction, and temporal precedence. Underline this idea.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
Two authorities are quoted: Imam Ali and Aristotle. Read their statements and ask what each adds to the argument. Ali points out that God is the origin of 'where' and 'how', so He is beyond such questions. Aristotle says divine knowledge requires a new 'nature' (fitrah) free from imagination.
The Aristotle quote is cited to show that human imagination is a barrier to understanding divine matters.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by declaring that the claim of self-evidence for denying an existent neither inside nor outside the world is false. Relate this back to the thesis: because the Creator is beyond space, He can exist without being spatially located.
This directly refutes the misconception that 'no inside nor outside' implies non-existence.

Source Text

يكون خالق الجهة والمدة منزها عن المباينة بالمكان والجهة ، ومنزها عن السبق على العالم بالزمان والمدة كان أولى. ولهذا قال الأمام الأجل علي بن أبي طالب عليه السلام : « الذي أين الأين. لا يقال له : أين ؟ والذي كيف الكيف ، لا يقال له : كيف ؟ ، ونقل عن الفيلسوف أرسطاطاليس أنه كتب في أول كتابه في الإلهيات : ( من أراد أن يشرع في المعارف الإلهية ، فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى )، ومراده : أن الإنسان ألف أحكام الوهم والخيال ، والمباحث الإلهية لا يوافقها أحكام الأوهام والخيالات. فلهذا السبب وجب على هذا الطالب استحداث فطرة أخرى. فهذا جملة الكلام في بيان أن ادعاء البديهة في نفي موجود ، لا يكون موجودا داخل العالم ولا خارجه : قول باطل .
The Creator of space and duration is transcendent of being separate by place and direction, and transcendent of preceding the world by time and duration was more appropriate. For this reason, the preeminent Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib, peace be upon him, said: 'He who (gives existence to) whereness; it is not said of Him: Where? And He who (gives existence to) howness; it is not said of Him: How?' And it is transmitted from the philosopher Aristotle that he wrote at the beginning of his book on theology: 'Whoever wants to engage in divine knowledge should acquire for himself another nature.' His meaning: that a person is accustomed to the judgments of imagination and fantasy, and divine inquiries do not accord with the judgments of imaginations and fantasies. For this reason, this seeker must acquire another nature. Commentary
This passage argues for God's absolute transcendence beyond space, time, and direction. The author begins by stating that the Creator, being the origin of space (jihah) and duration (muddah), is necessarily transcendent of separation by place and direction, and even more so of preceding the world by time. This is supported by two authoritative quotations: first, from Imam Ali, who uses the phrases 'the one who originates whereness' (alladhi ayna al-ayna) to indicate that God is beyond spatial questions; second, from Aristotle, who reportedly said that one must acquire a new nature (fitrah) for divine knowledge because ordinary human imagination is accustomed to spatial and temporal categories. The author then concludes that the claim that it is self-evidently impossible for an existent to be neither inside nor outside the world is therefore false, because such a claim relies on assumptions that do not apply to the transcendent Creator.

Refuting the Claim of Self-Evidence
The author summarizes and refutes the opponents' claim of self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponents' claim that 'God must be in a direction' is not self-evident and how the author refutes it.

Core Idea
Self-evidence cannot be claimed for a matter disputed by rational thinkers.

The author begins by summarizing the opponents' argument: that denying God a spatial location is equivalent to denying His existence entirely, and that this premise is self-evident. In this lesson, we'll see the author's first response: the claim of self-evidence is false because he has already provided ten proofs against it, and the matter is widely debated. We'll learn why dispute invalidates a claim of self-evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' argument
Read the passage where the author describes the opponents' reasoning: if God is neither inside nor outside the world, then He does not exist. This is the premise they claim is self-evident.
The opponents argue that the only way to negate existence is to negate location.
2
Identify the claim of self-evidence
Notice that the opponents assert their premise is 'بديهية' (self-evident), meaning it requires no proof and is immediately obvious.
3
Understand the author's refutation
The author refutes by stating that he has already shown through 'ten complete proofs' that this claim is false. He also points out that rational thinkers have disputed this matter, so it cannot be self-evident.
Self-evident truths cannot be rationally debated.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the existence of disagreement disproves self-evidence. If a proposition were truly self-evident, all rational people would accept it immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحيطة بنا . كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وحكما بكونه عدما محضا وسلبا صرفا . ولذلك فإنا إذا قلنا : إن فلانا لم يوجد في الدار ولم يوجد خارج الدار : كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وإذا كان لا طريق إلى تقرير النفي المحض والسلب الصرف إلا هذا ، ثبت أنا لو نفينا كونه تعالى على أحد هذه الوجوه فإنه يلزم منه نفي وجوده . فهذه جملة كلمات الخصوم في تقرير أن هذه المقدمة بديهية . واعلم أنه إن كان المقصود من هذه الكلمات : ادعاء أن إثبات موجود لا في داخل العالم ولا في خارج العالم قول معلوم الفساد بالضرورة والبديهة . فنحن قد بينا بالوجوه العشرة الكاملة أن ادعاء البديهة ههنا فاسد باطل . وإن كان المقصود منها ذكر الدليل على وجوب كون الله تعالى في الجهة . فنقول : هذا باطل لأن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الله تعالى غير مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . فهل يصح على هذا التقدير أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته ؟ لا أظن أن العاقل يحكم بصحة هذا التقسيم على تقدير سبق الاعتقاد بأنه مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الحق هو أنه تعالى خلق العالم لا في ذاته ولا مباينا عن ذاته بالحيز والجهة . فيثبت : أن قوله : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته، إنما يظهر كونه حقا بتقدير أن يثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى مختصا بالحيز والجهة. فلو أثبتنا صحة هذه المقدمة بالبناء على تلك المقدمة لزم توقيف الدليل على المدلول وتوقيف المدلول على الدليل ، وأنه يوجب الدور . والدور الباطل. فيثبت أنه إن كان مقصودهم من تلك الكلمات مجرد ادعاء البديهة فهو باطل قطعا . لأن الشيء المختلف فيه بين العقلاء قديما وحديثا . كيف يمكن ادعاء البديهة فيه ؟ لا سيما وجمهور العقلاء المحققين مصرون على فساده، وإما إن كان مقصودهم منه ذكر الدليل فهو يوجب الدور ، لأجل أن هذه المقدمة لا يمكن الاعتراف بصحتها إلا بعد إثبات هذا المطلوب ، فلو حاولنا إثبات هذا المطلوب بتلك المقدمة لزم الدور . وهذا كلام واف بالكشف عن حقيقة الحال في هذه المباحث .
What surrounds us. This was a negation of his existence, and a judgment that he is pure non-existence and absolute negation. Therefore, if we say: So-and-so does not exist in the house and does not exist outside the house: this is a negation of his existence. And if there is no way to establish pure negation and absolute denial except this, it is established that if we negate His being in one of these respects, then it necessarily entails the negation of His existence. This is the sum of the opponents' words in establishing that this premise is self-evident. Know that if the intent of these words is the claim that affirming an existent that is neither inside the world nor outside it is a statement known to be false by necessity and self-evidence, then we have already shown by the ten complete proofs that the claim of self-evidence here is false and invalid. And if the intent is to mention a proof for the necessity of God being in a direction, then we say: This is false because, assuming the truth is that God is not confined to space and direction at all, is it valid under this assumption to say that He created the world within Himself or outside Himself? I do not think a rational person would judge this division as valid under the assumption of prior belief that He is confined to space and direction at all. Because under this assumption, the truth is that He created the world neither within Himself nor separate from Himself by space and direction. So it is established that his statement: He created the world within Himself or outside Himself only appears true if it is established that He must be confined to space and direction. If we establish the validity of this premise by building on that premise, it would require making the proof dependent on the proven and the proven dependent on the proof, which necessitates circularity. And circularity is false. So it is established that if their intent is merely the claim of self-evidence, it is absolutely false, because how can self-evidence be claimed for something that has been disputed among rational people anciently and recently? Especially since the majority of rational investigators insist on its falsity. And if their intent is to mention a proof, it necessitates circularity, because this premise cannot be acknowledged as true except after establishing this desired conclusion. So if we try to establish this desired conclusion by that premise, circularity follows. And this speech is sufficient to uncover the reality of the situation in these investigations.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponents' argument that the statement "God is neither inside the world nor outside it" is self-evidently false or provable without circularity. The author begins by summarizing the opponents' claim: that denying God's existence in any spatial location amounts to denying His existence altogether, because the only way to negate something is to negate its location. The opponents assert that this premise is self-evident (بديهية). The author then presents a dilemma. If the opponents are claiming self-evidence, he says that he has already refuted this with ten complete proofs (الوجوه العشرة الكاملة), which established that claiming self-evidence here is false. Moreover, the fact that this matter is disputed among rational thinkers proves it cannot be self-evident. If, on the other hand, the opponents intend to provide a proof that God must be in a direction, then the author shows that such a proof leads to circularity (دور). He argues that to prove that God is in a direction, they need the premise that "God created the world either within Himself or outside Himself," but this division only makes sense if we already assume God is confined to space. Thus the proof relies on the very conclusion it seeks to prove, resulting in circular reasoning. The author concludes that either way—whether they claim self-evidence or proof—their position is invalid.

Key Terms
بديهية
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying spatial location to God is equivalent to denying His existence.
Correction: The author argues that this is not necessarily true; God exists without spatial confinement. The premise is not self-evident and is disputed.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the opponents' claim that their premise is self-evident?
A) It is accepted by all rational thinkers.
B) It is false and invalid, as he has shown with ten proofs.
C) It is a valid but unproven claim.
D) It is self-evident because it is based on common sense.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God must be in a direction is not self-evident, as it is disputed and has been refuted by multiple proofs.

The Circularity in the Opponents' Proof
The author refutes the opponents' proof by demonstrating circular reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how any proof that God must be in a direction leads to circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Any proof that God is in a direction presupposes the very conclusion it aims to establish.

The author now considers the second possibility: that the opponents might try to offer a proof rather than claim self-evidence. He shows that any such proof is circular. The key division they use—'God created the world within Himself or outside Himself'—only makes sense if we already assume God is confined to space. This lesson will unpack the circularity and why it invalidates the argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the hypothetical
The author sets a condition: suppose the truth is that God is not in any direction or space. Then consider the division: 'did He create the world within Himself or outside Himself?'
This division is only meaningful if God can be located.
2
Recognize the problem
The author argues that under the assumption of no spatial confinement, the division is false. The correct statement would be that God created the world neither inside nor outside Himself.
3
Identify the circularity
The opponents' proof for God being in a direction uses the division as a premise. But that premise itself relies on the conclusion (that God is in a direction). This creates a vicious circle.
Circularity (دور) is a logical fallacy.
4
Conclude the invalidity
Therefore, whether the opponents claim self-evidence or proof, their position is invalid. The author's refutation is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحيطة بنا . كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وحكما بكونه عدما محضا وسلبا صرفا . ولذلك فإنا إذا قلنا : إن فلانا لم يوجد في الدار ولم يوجد خارج الدار : كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وإذا كان لا طريق إلى تقرير النفي المحض والسلب الصرف إلا هذا ، ثبت أنا لو نفينا كونه تعالى على أحد هذه الوجوه فإنه يلزم منه نفي وجوده . فهذه جملة كلمات الخصوم في تقرير أن هذه المقدمة بديهية . واعلم أنه إن كان المقصود من هذه الكلمات : ادعاء أن إثبات موجود لا في داخل العالم ولا في خارج العالم قول معلوم الفساد بالضرورة والبديهة . فنحن قد بينا بالوجوه العشرة الكاملة أن ادعاء البديهة ههنا فاسد باطل . وإن كان المقصود منها ذكر الدليل على وجوب كون الله تعالى في الجهة . فنقول : هذا باطل لأن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الله تعالى غير مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . فهل يصح على هذا التقدير أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته ؟ لا أظن أن العاقل يحكم بصحة هذا التقسيم على تقدير سبق الاعتقاد بأنه مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الحق هو أنه تعالى خلق العالم لا في ذاته ولا مباينا عن ذاته بالحيز والجهة . فيثبت : أن قوله : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته، إنما يظهر كونه حقا بتقدير أن يثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى مختصا بالحيز والجهة. فلو أثبتنا صحة هذه المقدمة بالبناء على تلك المقدمة لزم توقيف الدليل على المدلول وتوقيف المدلول على الدليل ، وأنه يوجب الدور . والدور الباطل. فيثبت أنه إن كان مقصودهم من تلك الكلمات مجرد ادعاء البديهة فهو باطل قطعا . لأن الشيء المختلف فيه بين العقلاء قديما وحديثا . كيف يمكن ادعاء البديهة فيه ؟ لا سيما وجمهور العقلاء المحققين مصرون على فساده، وإما إن كان مقصودهم منه ذكر الدليل فهو يوجب الدور ، لأجل أن هذه المقدمة لا يمكن الاعتراف بصحتها إلا بعد إثبات هذا المطلوب ، فلو حاولنا إثبات هذا المطلوب بتلك المقدمة لزم الدور . وهذا كلام واف بالكشف عن حقيقة الحال في هذه المباحث .
What surrounds us. This was a negation of his existence, and a judgment that he is pure non-existence and absolute negation. Therefore, if we say: So-and-so does not exist in the house and does not exist outside the house: this is a negation of his existence. And if there is no way to establish pure negation and absolute denial except this, it is established that if we negate His being in one of these respects, then it necessarily entails the negation of His existence. This is the sum of the opponents' words in establishing that this premise is self-evident. Know that if the intent of these words is the claim that affirming an existent that is neither inside the world nor outside it is a statement known to be false by necessity and self-evidence, then we have already shown by the ten complete proofs that the claim of self-evidence here is false and invalid. And if the intent is to mention a proof for the necessity of God being in a direction, then we say: This is false because, assuming the truth is that God is not confined to space and direction at all, is it valid under this assumption to say that He created the world within Himself or outside Himself? I do not think a rational person would judge this division as valid under the assumption of prior belief that He is confined to space and direction at all. Because under this assumption, the truth is that He created the world neither within Himself nor separate from Himself by space and direction. So it is established that his statement: He created the world within Himself or outside Himself only appears true if it is established that He must be confined to space and direction. If we establish the validity of this premise by building on that premise, it would require making the proof dependent on the proven and the proven dependent on the proof, which necessitates circularity. And circularity is false. So it is established that if their intent is merely the claim of self-evidence, it is absolutely false, because how can self-evidence be claimed for something that has been disputed among rational people anciently and recently? Especially since the majority of rational investigators insist on its falsity. And if their intent is to mention a proof, it necessitates circularity, because this premise cannot be acknowledged as true except after establishing this desired conclusion. So if we try to establish this desired conclusion by that premise, circularity follows. And this speech is sufficient to uncover the reality of the situation in these investigations.

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponents' argument that the statement "God is neither inside the world nor outside it" is self-evidently false or provable without circularity. Moreover, the fact that this matter is disputed among rational thinkers proves it cannot be self-evident. If, on the other hand, the opponents intend to provide a proof that God must be in a direction, then the author shows that such a proof leads to circularity (دور). He argues that to prove that God is in a direction, they need the premise that "God created the world either within Himself or outside Himself," but this division only makes sense if we already assume God is confined to space. Thus the proof relies on the very conclusion it seeks to prove, resulting in circular reasoning. The author concludes that either way—whether they claim self-evidence or proof—their position is invalid.

Refuting Ibn Sina on Certainty and Imagination
The author refutes Ibn Sina's appeal to imagination via a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dilemma refutes Ibn Sina's claim that imagination provides certainty in theological premises.

Core Idea
Imagination cannot rival intellect; attributing certainty to it leads to self-destruction.

Ibn Sina conceded that certainty exists regarding the premise that an existent must be inside or outside the world, but he attributed this certainty to imagination rather than intellect. However, this concession opens the door to a powerful refutation. The author presents a dilemma that forces Ibn Sina's position into either circularity or irrelevance, showing why the premise must be rationally necessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn Sina's position
Read the opening lines where Ibn Sina accepts certainty but attributes it to imagination, and note his claim that imagination's judgments in non-sensible matters are unreliable.
This sets up the target of the refutation.
2
Understand the dilemma's first horn
Follow the first option: if imagination's certainty equals that of self-evident truths, then we face a contradiction. How can two equal certainties, one true and one false, coexist? The solution requires either necessary knowledge (which would prevent equal certainty) or proof, which leads to circularity.
The circularity is between self-evident truths and proof.
3
Understand the dilemma's second horn
Consider the second option: if imagination's certainty is weaker than rational certainty, then the premise is not certain from the start, so it loses all force.
Either way, Ibn Sina's position is undermined.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Notice that the author concludes the chapter, having shown that the premise must be a rational necessity, not grounded in imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي ابن سينا فإنه سلم أن الجزم بهذه المقدمة حاصل . إلا أنه زعم أن الجازم بها هو الوهم لا العقل . وزعم أن حكم الوهم في غير المحسوسات غير موثوق به . ولقائل أن يقول : إما أن تقولوا : إن الجزم الحاصل في هذه المقدمة يساوي الجزم الحاصل في سائر البديهيات . كقولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان . وإما أن تنكروا هذه المساواة . فإن ذهبتم إلى القسم الأول ، وهو : إذا كانت قوة الجزم في الصورتين بالتساوي . فالعلم يكون إحداهما حقة والأخرى باطلة ، إما أن يكون علما ضروريا أو نظريا. فإن كان العلم الضروري بالفرق بينها حاصلا ، كان ذلك مانعا من حصول الجزم القوي لكل واحد منهما ، وإن كان الفرق لا يعرف إلا بالنظر والدليل ، فحينئذ تصير صحة البديهيات موقوفة على الدليل ، إلا أن صحة الدليل موقوفة على البديهيات فيلزم الدور وهو باطل . وإما إن ذهبتم إلى القسم الثاني وهو أن الجزم بهذه القضية ليس في القوة والشدة مثل جزم العقل بالبديهيات فحينئذ تخرج هذه القضية عن أن يكون مجزوما بها ابتداء . وذلك يوجب سقوطها بالكلية . فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب .
As for the chief master Abu Ali Ibn Sina, he conceded that certainty about this premise is obtained. However, he claimed that what produces certainty is imagination (الوهم), not intellect. And he claimed that the judgment of imagination in non-sensible matters is unreliable. But one may say: Either you say that the certainty obtained about this premise equals the certainty obtained about other self-evident truths, like our saying: one is half of two, and negation and affirmation cannot coexist and cannot both be absent; or you deny this equality. If you adopt the first division, namely that the strength of certainty in the two cases is equal, then knowledge, one of them being true and the other false, must be either necessary or acquired. If the necessary knowledge of the difference between them is present, that would prevent the strong certainty about each one; but if the difference is known only through reflection and proof, then the validity of self-evident truths becomes dependent on proof, but the validity of proof is dependent on self-evident truths, leading to circularity, which is false. And if you adopt the second division, namely that certainty about this proposition is not as strong and intense as intellect's certainty about self-evident truths, then this proposition ceases to be certain from the outset. That necessitates its complete collapse. This completes the discussion of this chapter.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's position and its refutation. Ibn Sina accepts that certainty (الجزم) about the premise (that an existent cannot be neither inside nor outside the world) is obtained, but he attributes this certainty to the imagination (الوهم) rather than the intellect. He argues that the judgment of imagination in non-sensible matters is unreliable. The author then presents a counter-argument in the form of a dilemma. The opponent (Ibn Sina or his follower) is asked: do you claim that the certainty from imagination equals the certainty of self-evident truths (بديهيات) like "one is half of two" and the law of non-contradiction, or do you admit it is weaker? If you claim equality, then since we have two contradictory certainties (one true, one false), either we have necessary knowledge to distinguish them, in which case we would not have equal certainty in both, or we need reflection and proof, which leads to circularity because the proof depends on self-evident truths and self-evident truths depend on proof. If you claim the certainty is weaker, then the premise is not certain initially, so it loses all force. Thus, Ibn Sina's position is refuted either way. The author concludes the chapter.

Key Terms
وهم
بديهيات
جزم
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagination can provide reliable certainty for non-sensible premises.
Correction: The passage shows that if imagination gives certainty equal to rational self-evidence, it leads to circularity; if weaker, the premise loses all force. So imagination cannot be a source of certainty for such premises.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what happens if the certainty from imagination is admitted to be weaker than that of self-evident truths?
A) The premise becomes more certain due to humility.
B) The premise loses all certainty and collapses.
C) It leads to circular reasoning.
D) It confirms imagination as a valid faculty.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's attempt to base certainty on imagination fails because it either leads to circular reasoning or collapses under its own weakness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If that belief could not withstand the dilemma of equality or inequality, would your faith be shaken or refined?
?

Are you prepared to defend your most foundational beliefs with such logical precision, or do you rely on comfortable feelings?

First Proof: Intuitive Reason Accepts Unique Existence
First proof that lack of a likeness does not entail non-existence, based on intuitive reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof from intuitive reason establishes that the absence of a likeness does not necessitate non-existence.

Core Idea
If intuitive reason doesn't reject a unique existent, nothing is lost without a like.

The author begins the chapter by stating the thesis: it is not necessary for every existent to have a counterpart, and denying a likeness does not deny existence itself. This lesson presents the first proof, which appeals to your immediate, intuitive reason. Before diving into complex theological arguments, Sanusi grounds the discussion in a simple truth you already recognize naturally.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the English translation. Let the rhythm of the argument settle in your mind.
Note that the Arabic contains some uncertain words; focus on the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the thesis
The passage opens with a double claim: not every existent must have a like, and denying a like does not negate existence. Underline this in your mind.
3
Understand the role of intuitive reason
The proof uses 'badīhat al-ʿaql' – immediate, self-evident reason. It asks: does reason instinctively reject the idea of a unique being with unique attributes? The answer is no.
The Arabic says 'لا تستبعد' – does not deem improbable. This is a key term.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since intuitive reason does not find uniqueness impossible, we conclude that lacking a like does not entail lacking existence. This refutes the common misunderstanding.
The conclusion is stated explicitly: 'لا يلزم من عدم نظير الشيء، عدم ذلك الشيء'.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Some think that if something has no equal, it cannot be real. This proof shows that such a belief is not rationally mandatory; it is not a self-evident truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أنه لا يجب أن يكون لكل موجوده فطيرة [؟] نظیر و شبیه وانه ليس يلزم من نفي النظير والتشبيه نفي ذلك البيئي [؟] الذي يدل على صحة قولنا وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إن بديمة العقل لا تستبعد وجود موجود موصوف بصفات مخصوصة ، بحيث يكون كل ما سواه مخالفا له في تلك الخصوصية . وإذا لم يكن هذا ممتنعاً في البديهة ، علمنا : أنه لا يلزم من عدم نظير الشيء ، عدم ذلك الشيء
Chapter Two: On explaining that it is not necessary for every existent to have a counterpart and likeness, and that the negation of likeness and similitude does not entail the negation of that thing which indicates the validity of our statement. The first proof: Indeed, intuitive reason (badīhat al-ʿaql) does not deem improbable the existence of an existent described with specific attributes, such that everything else is opposed to it in that specificity. And since this is not impossible in intuition (al-badīha), we know that the absence of a likeness of a thing does not necessitate the absence of that thing.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage opens Chapter Two with the thesis: it is not necessary for every existent to have a counterpart or likeness, and denying a likeness does not entail denying the thing itself. The first proof appeals to intuitive reason (badīhat al-ʿaql). It argues that reason does not instinctively reject the possibility of an existent possessing unique attributes such that everything else differs from it in that specific respect. Since this is not deemed impossible by immediate intuition, we conclude that the lack of a like (naẓīr) for a thing does not imply that the thing itself does not exist. The proof is based on the absence of a self-evident contradiction.

النظير
التشبيه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if something has no like, it cannot exist.
Correction: This proof shows that such a belief is not supported by intuitive reason; reason does not find uniqueness impossible, so the absence of a like does not imply non-existence.

D) The existence of multiple things.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive reason does not reject the concept of a unique existent, so lacking a like does not imply non-existence.

Third Proof: Unique Determination Cannot Be Shared
Third proof that determination is unique and cannot be shared.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific determination of a thing is unique to it and cannot be shared, and how this leads to the conclusion that the absence of a like does not negate existence.

Core Idea
A thing's individual determination is exclusive to it; no other can possess it.

This passage presents the third proof in a series arguing for the uniqueness of divine attributes. The author distinguishes between the shared essence common to individuals of a kind and the specific determination that makes each individual itself. The argument shows that this determination is impossible to find in another, thereby demonstrating that the absence of a like is irrelevant to a thing's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
2
Distinguish essence from determination
Notice the key distinction between الماهية المشترك (shared essence) and التعين (specific determination). The proof hinges on this difference.
Essence is what is common; determination is what individuates.
3
Follow the logical implication
The author argues that if determination could be found in another, that other would be the same thing itself – an absurdity. Grasp why this is intuitively impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since determination is unique, the absence of a like or equal does not imply the thing does not exist. This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن تعين كل شيء من حيث أنه هو هو ، مغاير لأصل الماهية المشترك فيها ، بين تلك الأشخاص . ثم نقول : إن تعين هذا الشيء المعين، ممتنع الحصول في غيره [ وإلا لكان ذلك الشيء نفس غيره ، وذلك باطل في بديهة العقل فيثبت : أن تعين كل شيء من حيث إنه هو ممتنع الحصول في غيره ] فعلمنا : أن عدم النظير والمساوي لا يدل على عدم الشيء . فظهر
The third proof: The determination (taʿayyun) of each thing, inasmuch as it is itself, is distinct from the essence (al-māhiyya) common among those individuals. Then we say: The determination of this specific thing is impossible to obtain in another, [otherwise that thing would be the same as another, and that is invalid in intuitive reason. Thus it is established: The determination of each thing inasmuch as it is itself is impossible to obtain in another.] So we know that the absence of a like and equal does not indicate the absence of the thing. So it is clear.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. It distinguishes between the shared essence (al-māhiyya al-mushtaraka) that is common among individuals of a kind, and the specific determination (taʿayyun) that makes each individual itself. The proof argues that the determination of a particular thing cannot be found in another, because if it could, that other would be the same thing itself, which is intuitively absurd. Therefore, the uniqueness of a thing does not require it to have a like; rather, its determination is exclusive to it. Consequently, the absence of a likeness or equal does not entail the non-existence of the thing.

Key Terms
التعين
الماهية
النظير
المساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if something has no like or equal, it must not exist.
Correction: The proof shows that the absence of a like is not evidence for non-existence, because determination is unique and cannot be shared.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof identify as unique to each thing and incapable of being found in another?
A) Its essence (ماهية)
B) Its attributes (صفات)
C) Its specific determination (تعين)
D) Its existence (وجود)

Key Takeaway
The specific determination of each thing is uniquely its own and cannot be shared; therefore, the lack of a like does not negate existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the determination of each thing is exclusive and cannot be shared, then your very existence as a unique individual is a sign of the Creator's wisdom. But have you ever acted as if your purpose could be found by imitating others, rather than fulfilling your own unique determination? What would change if you took this truth seriously?
?
The proof shows that absence of a like does not indicate non-existence. In your life, how often do you doubt your own worth or the reality of your faith simply because you don't see others like you? Are you willing to stand firm in your convictions even if you stand alone, based on the certainty of your own determination?
?
The source argues that if a determination could exist in another, that other would be identical. This is absurd. Reflect: Do you ever try to claim the identity or accomplishments of others as your own, forgetting that your own determination is uniquely given? What would it mean for you to fully embrace your distinct purpose?

Concluding Refutation: No Counterpart Required
Sanusi refutes the view that conceiving an existent without spatial relation requires a counterpart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the absence of a counterpart does not negate the existence of a unique transcendent being, and how Sanusi refutes the opposing view.

Core Idea
No counterpart? No problem — existence does not depend on having an equal.

Sanusi concludes his proof by addressing a common objection: how can we conceive of a being that is neither connected to nor separate from the world? Some argue that such a conception requires a counterpart. Sanusi shows this requirement is baseless, and the argument collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: premise, application, conclusion.
The Arabic is concise; the translation captures the key terms.
2
Identify the opposing claim
Find the specific claim Sanusi is refuting: that we cannot conceive an existent without spatial relation unless we have a counterpart.
Look for the phrase 'إنه لا علينا أن نعقل' – it introduces the opposing view.
3
Understand the refutation
See how Sanusi uses the previous proofs (that absence of counterpart does not imply non-existence) to reject the opposing claim. The concluding phrase 'entirely invalid' shows the decisive nature of the refutation.
Sanusi asserts that only God has the attribute of being neither connected nor separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلمنا : أن عدم النظير والمساوي لا يدل على عدم الشيء . فظهر بهذا فساد قول من يقول : إنه لا علينا أن نعقل وجود موجود لا يكون متصلاً بالعالم ولا منفصلاً عنه ، إلا إذا وجدنا له نظيراً . فإن عندنا الموصوف بهذه الصفة ليس إلا الله تعالى. وبينا : أنه لا يلزم من عدم النظير أو الشبيه ، عدم ذلك الشيء . فيثبت : أن هذا الكلام ساقط بالكلية
Thus we know: the non-existence of a counterpart and equal does not indicate the non-existence of the thing. So by this, the falsity of the statement of those who say: 'It is not upon us that we conceive an existent that is neither connected to the world nor separate from it, unless we find for it a counterpart' becomes clear. For according to us, the one described with this attribute is none other than God Almighty. And we have shown that the non-existence of a counterpart or similar does not entail the non-existence of that thing. So it is established that this statement is entirely invalid.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the concluding refutation. Sanusi summarizes the lesson from the three proofs: the absence of a counterpart or equal does not prove the non-existence of a thing. He then targets a specific opposing view: that we cannot rationally conceive an existent that is neither connected to the world nor separate from it (i.e., transcendent) unless we find a counterpart for it. For Sanusi, the only being described with such attributes is God. Since the proofs have shown that lacking a counterpart does not negate existence, the opposing argument collapses entirely. The refutation is decisive: the statement is 'entirely invalid' (sāqiṭ bil-kulliyya).

Key Terms
نظير (counterpart)
شبيه (similar)
متصل بالعالم (connected to the world)
منفصل (separate)
ساقط بالكلية (entirely invalid)

Misconception: One might think that transcendent beings like God must have analogues to be conceivable.
Correction: Sanusi denies this necessity; the absence of a counterpart does not make a being inconceivable.

Key Takeaway
The absence of a counterpart does not prove non-existence; therefore, God's uniqueness does not make Him inconceivable.

Refutation of the Need for a Parallel
Refutes the argument that an existent must have a parallel to be conceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that an existent must have a parallel to be conceivable, and why uniqueness does not imply incomprehensibility.

Core Idea
A unique being needs no parallel to be understood; Allah's uniqueness does not negate His existence.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection: that we can only conceive of something if it has a parallel in our experience. This argument is used to deny the possibility of a transcendent being. The author refutes it by pointing out that Allah is the only being without connection or separation, and lacking a parallel does not make Him impossible.

Opinions on God's Corporeality and Form
Classifies two major opinions on God's body and details anthropomorphist views on His form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of opinions on God's corporeality and form, distinguishing between the majority who affirm transcendence (tanzih) and the minority who affirm anthropomorphism (tashbih), including the variations among anthropomorphists.

Core Idea
The debate over God's form is a clash between tanzih (transcendence) and tashbih (anthropomorphism).

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyyah presents a concise map of the major theological positions on God's corporeality and form. The author lays out the consensus of the majority on tanzih, then explains the internal divisions among anthropomorphists, including a comparison between Muslim and Jewish anthropomorphic traditions. Understanding this classification is essential for later refutations of such views.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two major opinions
Read the passage and note that the author states two main opinions: the majority affirms God's transcendence (tanzih) over body and location, while the minority (mujassima) affirm that He is corporeal and located.
2
Examine the internal divisions among corporealists
Among those who say God is a body, they differ on form. Some say He is in human form, others do not. Among those who affirm human form, there are two transmitted views: Muslim anthropomorphists say He is like a young man; Jewish anthropomorphists say He is like an old man.
The author is reporting, not endorsing.
3
Analyze the footnote
Read the footnote containing Torah verses and Maimonides' interpretation. Notice how it provides scriptural evidence and an interpretive principle that anthropomorphic language is metaphorical.
The footnote supports the author's point that anthropomorphism appears in scriptures but requires ta'wil.
4
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Consider that the author classifies these opinions to set up a later refutation. He is not asserting these views as true but presenting the landscape of positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في إقامة الدلائل على أنه تعالى يمتنع أن يكون حينما لأهل العالم في هذا الباب قولان : فالجمهور الأعظم منهم اتفقوا على تنزيه الله سبحانه وتعالى عن الجسمية ، والحصول في الحيز . وقال الباقون : إنه متحيز ، وحاصل في الحيز . وهؤلاء هم المجسمة . ثم القائلون بأنه جسم اختلفوا في أشياء . فالأول : إنهم في الصورة على قولين . منهم من قال : إنه على صورة الإنسان . ومنهم من لا يقول به . أما الأول فالمنقول عن مشبهة المسلمين : أنه تعالى على صورة إنسان شاب . والمنقول عن مشبهة اليهود (۱) : إنه على صورة إنسان شيخ . (1) في التوراة أن الله إله واحد . وفي التوراة أن الله ليس كمثله شيء . ففي الأصحاح السادس من سفر التثنية : ( اسمع يا إسرائيل . إن الرب إلهنا رب واحد . فأحبب الرب إلهك بكل قلبك وكل نفسك وكل قدرتك . ولتكن هذه الكلمات التي أنا أمرك بها اليوم في قلبك ، وكررها على بنيك وكلمهم بها إذا جلست في بيتك وإذا مشيت في الطريق وإذا لمت وإذا قمت . واعقدها علامة على يدك ، ولتكن عصائب بين عينيك ، واكتبها على عضائد أبواب بيتك وعلى أبوابك . تثنية ٦ : ٤-٩ ترجمة اليسوعيين ] وفي الأصحاح الثالث والثلاثين من سفر التثنية : ولا كفه الله ، وفي ترجمة البروتستانت : و ليس مثل الله : [ تثنية ٣٣ : ٢٦ ] . وقال موسى بن ميمون في دلالة الحائرين : إن ما ورد في التوراة مما يوحي بأن الله شبه إنسان بأعضائه وبصفاته ، فذلك مؤول على معنى : إن الله يقرب ذاته إلى عقول الخلق ، حديثه عن نفسه كأنه واحد منهم. أما هو فليس مثل انسان وليس كمثله شيء وذلك ليفهم الخلق ذات الله .
Chapter Three: On establishing proofs that He, exalted, is impossible to be... The people of the world have two opinions on this matter: The great majority of them agreed on declaring Allah, glorified and exalted, transcendent (tanzīh) from corporeality and from being in a location. And the rest said: He is located in a place and occupies a place. These are the corporealists (mujassima). Then those who say He is a body differed on various points. The first: They are on two opinions regarding form. Some of them said: He is in the form of a human. And some of them do not say that. As for the former, what is transmitted from the Muslim anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) is that He, exalted, is in the form of a young human. And what is transmitted from the Jewish anthropomorphists is that He is in the form of an old human. (1) In the Torah, God is one God. And in the Torah, nothing is like Him. In the sixth chapter of Deuteronomy: 'Hear, O Israel: The Lord our God is one Lord. Love the Lord your God with all your heart and with all your soul and with all your strength. These commandments that I give you today are to be in your heart. Impress them on your children. Talk about them when you sit at home and when you walk along the road, when you lie down and when you get up. Tie them as symbols on your hands and bind them on your foreheads. Write them on the doorframes of your houses and on your gates.' Deuteronomy 6:4-9, Jesuit translation. And in the thirty-third chapter of Deuteronomy: 'There is none like God' (Protestant translation: 'There is no one like God' Deuteronomy 33:26). And Moses Maimonides said in the Guide of the Perplexed: What is in the Torah that suggests that God resembles a human in his limbs and attributes is interpreted to mean that God brings His essence close to the minds of creatures, speaking of Himself as if He were one of them. But He is not like a human, and nothing is like Him, so that creatures may understand God's essence.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a classification of opinions on God's corporeality. The author begins Chapter Three on establishing proofs. He states that people have two main opinions: the majority affirm God's transcendence (tanzīh) over body and location; the remainder (mujassima) affirm that He is located and corporeal. Then among those who say He is a body, they differ on details. First, regarding form, there are two opinions: some say He is in human form, others disagree. Among those who say human form, Muslim anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) transmit that He is like a young man, while Jewish anthropomorphists transmit that He is like an old man. A long footnote then provides scriptural evidence from the Torah and cites Maimonides' interpretation that anthropomorphic expressions are metaphorical to aid human understanding. This footnote supports the author's point that even scriptural traditions contain anthropomorphism that requires interpretation.

The First Proof: The Necessary Existent Cannot Be a Body
Razi presents the first rational proof that God is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's first proof that God is not a body, including the premises and their justifications.

Core Idea
The Necessary Existent cannot be contingent, and every body is contingent, so God is not a body.

This lesson introduces the first proof from Fakhr al-Din al-Razi demonstrating God's transcendence beyond corporeality. The argument is a crisp syllogism that relies on the fundamental classifications of existence: necessary and possible, and the notion of composition. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping the Islamic theological commitment to God's simplicity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the syllogistic structure and the use of terms like واجب الوجود لذاته and متحيز.
The passage includes a bracketed clarification embedded in the main text.
2
Identify the main syllogism
The proof is a categorical syllogism with two premises and a conclusion. Find the minor premise, major premise, and conclusion.
3
Examine the justification for the major premise
Razi argues that every spatially extended thing is composite, and every composite is contingent. Identify the sub-argument and the two reasons given for why every spatial thing is composite.
The first reason uses the distinction between right and left sides.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this proof matter? It establishes that God cannot be a body, which is a core tenet of Islamic theology. Consider how this fits into the broader discussion of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على أنه تعالى منزه عن الجسمية والحجمية وجوه : الحجة الأولى : لا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بممكن الوجود لذاته ، وكل متحيز فإنه ممكن الوجود لذاته، ينتج فلا شيء من واجب الوجود لذاته بمتحيز [ أما الصغرى فبديهية ، وأما الكبرى فلأن كل متحيز مركب ، وكل مرکب ممكن لذاته، ينتج : ان كل متحيز ممكن لذاته ](٣) وإنما قلنا : إن كل متحيز مركب لوجوه : الأول : إن كل متحيز فإن يمينه مغاير ليساره ، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو مركب ، ينتج : أن كل متحيز مركب . وتمام القول فيه مقرر بالدلائل المذكورة في نفي الجوهر الفرد والثاني : قالت الفلاسفة : كل جسم فهو مركب من الهيولى والصورة .
And what indicates that He, exalted, is free from corporeality and magnitude are several proofs: The first proof: Nothing that is necessary of existence by itself is possible of existence by itself, and every spatially extended thing is possible of existence by itself, so it follows that nothing that is necessary of existence by itself is spatially extended. [As for the minor premise, it is self-evident; as for the major premise, it is because every spatially extended thing is composite, and every composite is possible by itself, so it follows that every spatially extended thing is possible by itself.](3) And we only say that every spatially extended thing is composite due to several reasons: The first: Every spatially extended thing has a right side different from its left side, and everything that is like that is composite, so it follows that every spatially extended thing is composite. The full discussion of this is established by the proofs mentioned in the refutation of the indivisible atom. The second: The philosophers said: Every body is composed of hyle and form.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage begins a section of proofs that God is free from body and magnitude. Razi states that there are several proofs, and then presents the first. The proof is a categorical syllogism: (1) Nothing that is necessary of existence by itself (واجب الوجود لذاته) is possible of existence by itself (possible in itself, i.e., contingent). (2) Every spatially extended thing (متحيز) is possible of existence by itself. Conclusion: Therefore, nothing necessary of existence by itself is spatially extended. Razi then clarifies that the minor premise is self-evident: the Necessary Existent cannot be contingent. The major premise is justified by saying that every spatially extended thing is composite, and every composite is contingent. So the chain is: every thing spatial → composite → contingent. To defend that every spatial thing is composite, Razi gives two reasons. The first reason: every spatial thing has distinguishable right and left sides, and that entails composition (since different parts). He refers to fuller proofs elsewhere about the indivisible atom. The second reason (which is begun but not completed in this window) cites the philosophers who say every body is composed of matter (hyle) and form. Thus the first proof establishes God's non-corporeality.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود لذاته
ممكن الوجود لذاته
متحيز
مركب
يمين
يسار
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the proof assumes that every composite is spatial, but it argues the opposite: spatial implies composite.
Correction: The proof is that spatial extension implies composition, not the converse.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, why is every spatially extended thing considered composite?
A) Because it has a right side different from its left side.
B) Because it is possible of existence.
C) Because it occupies space.
D) Because it is created.

Key Takeaway
The first proof rationally establishes God's transcendence of body by showing that the Necessary Existent cannot be contingent, and every body is contingent due to its composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just reasoned that God cannot be a body because every body is composite and contingent. Yet, deep down, do you ever catch yourself imagining God as being 'somewhere' or having a form? How does this proof confront that tendency? Take a moment to ask Allah to purify your heart from any anthropomorphic images, and commit to repeating this rational tawḥīd silently in your mind when you pray.

Razi uses logic to defend divine transcendence. Are you willing to let your intellect submit to revelation and rational proofs, even when they challenge comfortable, human-like pictures of God? What is one specific step you can take this week to deepen your understanding of God's attributes through study, rather than relying solely on emotions?
?
If you truly believed that God is beyond all spatial extension, how would it change your awe of Him? Would you still feel close to a God who is not 'somewhere'? Reflect on the verse 'He is with you wherever you are' (Quran 57:4) and ask yourself: does this reality require distance or closeness? Let this question push you to rely more on God's knowledge and presence than on a physical sense of proximity.

Two Disputed Positions on God's Spatiality and Attributes
The author lists two disputed issues regarding God's location and the modality of His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two disputed positions about God's spatial existence and attributes that the author enumerates before refuting them.

Core Idea
Two disputes among corporealists: how God occupies space and how attributes inhere in His body.

This passage presents two specific theological disagreements that arise from the assumption that God is a corporeal being occupying space. The author lists them as positions held by others, which he will later refute. Understanding these positions is crucial for following his arguments for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the numbered topics and the structure of each dispute.
The passage is concise; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the two topics
The sixth topic asks whether God occupies a specific space by His essence or by an attribute. The seventh topic asks whether attributes are evenly distributed in the body or each has a specific locus.
The author compares the first dispute to the earlier debate about knowledge by essence or by knowledge.
3
Recognize the author's stance
The author is not endorsing these views; he is listing them as positions to be refuted. The phrase 'to each of these two opinions a proponent has gone' indicates they are held by others.
This sets the stage for later arguments against corporealism.
4
Consider the underlying assumption
Both disputes assume God is a body occupying space. The author will argue that God is transcendent above such limitations.
Understanding the assumption helps you see why these views are problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموضع السادس : إنه تعالى حاصل في ذلك الحيز المعين لذاته . أو لأجل معنى قائم به ، يقتضي حصوله في الجهة المعينة ؟ وهو مثل اختلافهم في أنه تعالى عالم لذاته، أو عالم بالعلم ؟ وهذا هو التنبيه على مواضع الخلاف والوفاق . الموضع السابع : إن العلم والقدرة والإرادة والسمع والبصر والكلام ، حالة في جميع أجزاء ذلك الجسم بالسوية ، أو يكون لكل واحد من هذه الصفات جزء معين من ذلك الجسم يكون ذلك الحيز محلا لتلك الصفة بعينها ؟ ذهب إلى كل واحد من هذين القولين ذاهب .
The sixth topic: whether the Exalted exists in that specific space by His essence, or due to a meaning subsisting in Him that necessitates His occurrence in that specific direction. This is like their disagreement about whether He is knower by His essence or knower by knowledge. This is an indication of the points of agreement and disagreement. The seventh topic: whether knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, and speech exist in all parts of that body equally, or whether each of these attributes has a specific part of that body that serves as a locus for that very attribute. To each of these two opinions a proponent has gone.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage lists two points of theological disagreement (mawāḍiʿ al-khilāf) concerning God’s corporeality. The sixth topic asks whether God, if occupying a particular space (al-ḥayz al-muʿayyan), does so by His own essence or due to some attribute that necessitates that location. This mirrors the earlier debate about whether God is knower by essence or by knowledge. The seventh topic asks whether the divine attributes (knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech) are uniformly distributed throughout the particles of the assumed body, or each attribute is tied to a specific part. The author notes that each of these positions has its proponents. The listing serves to frame the subsequent proofs that God is free from corporeality and spatiality.

Correction: The author is stating views held by others, which he intends to refute. He does not endorse corporealism.

The Second Proof: Neither Necessary Nor Contingent Attributes
Second proof against God being a body by analyzing necessary/contingent attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalām argument against God being a body, which shows that God's attributes cannot be necessary or contingent in a corporeal being.

Core Idea
If God were a body, His attributes would be either necessary or contingent—both lead to absurdity.

This passage continues the polemic against corporealism. The first proof (not shown) established that the Creator cannot be a body. Here, a second, more subtle argument is presented: it examines the attributes of a hypothetical divine body. By analyzing whether those attributes are necessary or contingent, the argument forces the conclusion that the cause of those attributes cannot itself be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the literal translation. Try to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
2
Identify the dilemma
The argument presents a dichotomy: the attributes of the supposed divine body are either necessary or contingent. Locate this split in the text.
Necessary = attributes that must be present; contingent = attributes that could be absent.
3
Trace each branch's refutation
Notice why necessary attributes are impossible (they would force all bodies to have them) and why contingent attributes also fail (they require an external cause leading to regression).
4
Understand the conclusion
The argument concludes that the maker of the attributes cannot be a body; hence the Creator is non-corporeal.
This directly supports the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخالق الكل يمتنع أن يكون متحيزاً . الثاني : إن اختصاص ذلك الجسم بالعلم والقدرة والإلهية إما أن يكون من الواجبات ، أو من الجائزات . والأول باطل ، وإلا لزم أن تكون كل الأجسام موصوفة بتلك الصفات على سبيل الوجوب، لما أنه ثبت : أن الأفراد الداخلة تحت النوع يجب كونها متساوية في جميع اللوازم ، والثاني باطل . وإلا لزم أن لا يحصل في ذلك الجسم المعين : هذه الصفات . إلا بجعل جاعل وتخصيص مخصص . فإن كان ذلك الجاعل جسماً ، عاد الكلام فيه . ولزم إما التسلل وإما الدور . وإن لم يكن جسماً فهو المطلوب .
So the Creator of all is impossible to be a body. The second: Indeed the specification of that body with knowledge, power, and divinity is either from the necessary things or from the contingent things. The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate that all bodies are described with those attributes necessarily, because it is established that the individuals falling under the species must be equal in all concomitants. And the second is false, otherwise it would necessitate that these attributes are not attained in that specific body except by a maker and a specifier. If that maker is a body, the discussion returns to it, and either infinite regress or circularity would follow. And if it is not a body, then that is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage opens with the assertion that the Creator of all cannot be a body. It then presents the second proof (the first is assumed from a previous discussion). The argument takes a hypothetical body claimed to be God and examines its attributes (knowledge, power, divinity). It divides the possibilities: either these attributes are necessary for that body, or they are contingent. If necessary, then all bodies would share them, which is false. If contingent, they require an external cause (a maker). If that cause is also a body, the same question recurs, leading to infinite regress or circularity. Therefore, the cause must be non‑corporeal, proving the original claim.

Key Terms
متحيز
واجب
جائز
تسلل
دور
مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument assumes God is a body and then tries to prove He isn't; actually it refutes the assumption by showing absurd consequences.
Correction: The argument uses a hypothetical body claimed to be God and shows that its attributes lead to contradictions, thus proving the original assumption false.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, if the attributes of a body claimed to be God were necessary, what would follow?
A) That body would be unique.
B) All bodies would have those attributes necessarily.
C) The attributes would be contingent.
D) The maker would be a body.

Key Takeaway
The second proof demonstrates that a corporeal deity is rationally impossible because its attributes would either be necessary (and thus shared by all bodies) or contingent (requiring an external cause, leading to regress or non-corporeality).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If the cause of God's attributes were another body, we would be trapped in an endless chain. Where in your life do you rely on finite causes instead of the First Cause? Can you commit today to orient your trust solely on the non-corporeal Creator?

The Third Proof: Similar Bodies Cannot Mix Eternal and Temporal
Argues that if all bodies are similar, none can be eternal while others are temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third kalām proof against divine corporeality, which argues that if all bodies are similar, none can be eternal while others are temporal.

Core Idea
If any body were eternal, all bodies could be eternal, leading to absurdity.

This lesson presents the third rational proof used by kalām theologians to demonstrate that Allah cannot be a body. The argument relies on the principle of similarity: since all bodies share the same essential nature, if one body were eternal, then any body could be eternal — which contradicts the definition of eternity as unchanging. Let’s examine this concise but powerful proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the use of 'بديهة' (intuition) for final certainty.
2
Identify the premise and supposition
The argument starts with the premise that all bodies are similar (متماثلة). Then it supposes (فلو فرضنا) that some are eternal and some originated. Follow the logical steps.
3
Apply the principle of similarity
The key principle: whatever is true of one thing is true of its like. Apply this to the supposition: if one body is eternal, any like body could also be eternal. That leads to the possibility of change.
This is the core of the proof.
4
See the absurd conclusion
The possibility of the eternal turning originated and vice versa is impossible (محال) by intuition. Therefore, no body can be eternal. Hence, God, who is eternal, cannot be a body.
The proof ends with 'بديهة' indicating self-evident impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : [ إن الأجسام (۱) ] لما كانت متماثلة ، فلو فرضنا بعضها قديماً ، وبعضها محدثاً لزم المحال . ذلك لأن كل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله . فيلزم جواز أن ينقلب القديم محدثاً ، وأن ينقلب المحدث قديماً . وذلك محال ، معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة
And the third: [Indeed bodies (1)] since they are similar, so if we suppose some of them eternal and some originated, the impossible would follow. That is because whatever is true of a thing is true of its like. Hence it would necessitate the possibility of the eternal turning into originated and the originated turning into eternal. That is impossible, known to be impossible by intuition.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This is the third proof against divine corporeality. It begins by stating that all bodies are similar (mumāthala). If one body were eternal (qadīm) and another originated (muḥdath), then by the principle that whatever holds for one holds for its like, the eternal body could become originated and vice versa. That would mean an eternal being, by definition uncaused and unchanging, could change temporally, which is absurd. Therefore, no body can be eternal; hence God, who is eternal, cannot be a body.

Key Terms
متماثلة (similar)
قديم (eternal)
محدث (originated)
بديهة (intuition)
مثل (like)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the third proof conclude about the possibility of a body being eternal?
A) A body can be eternal if it is unique.
B) No body can be eternal because of similarity of bodies.
C) Only some bodies can be eternal.
D) The proof does not address eternality.

Key Takeaway
Since all bodies are similar, if one were eternal, all could be, which is impossible; therefore, no body is eternal, and God cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You say you believe Allah is not a body, but when you think of His 'hand' or 'throne', do you truly guard your heart from imagining something physical? What specific thought will you correct today?
?

?
The proof forces you to rely on logical certainty. Are you willing to let your mind be guided by such proofs, even when they challenge your emotional or habitual images of Allah? What one step will you take this week to strengthen your intellectual conviction in His transcendence?

The Fourth Proof: Bodies Undergo Dispersion and Decay
Shows that all bodies are subject to dispersion, increase, and decay, so God cannot be a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof demonstrates that God cannot be a body because all bodies are subject to dispersion and decay.

Core Idea
Everything that can break apart cannot be God.

This passage presents the fourth argument in a series of proofs denying corporeality of God. By observing that all bodies share certain properties—like the possibility of dispersion and decay—the author shows that any body, including one claimed to be divine, would necessarily be subject to those same limitations. The conclusion is clear: these properties are incompatible with divinity, so God is not a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the original Arabic and its translation to grasp the argument's structure.
Pay attention to the parallel phrasing: 'just as... so must...'
2
Identify the key properties
List the six properties that are possible for all bodies: dispersion, tearing, increase, decrease, decay, corruption.
These are divided into two groups in the text.
3
Understand the conclusion
Notice how the author uses analogy to show that if God were a body, He would share these imperfect properties, which is impossible.
The final phrase 'that is false and impossible' refers to the idea that God could be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : إنه كما صح التفرق والتمزق على سائر الأجسام ، وجب أن يصحا على ذلك الجسم ، وكما صحت الزيادة والنقصان ، والعفونة والفساد على سائر الأجسام ، وجب أن يصح كل ذلك عليه . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل محال .
And the fourth: Just as dispersion and tearing are possible for all other bodies, they must be possible for that body; and just as increase and decrease, decay and corruption are possible for all other bodies, all of that must be possible for it. And it is known that this is false and impossible.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This fourth proof draws an analogy between the hypothetical body claimed to be God and all other bodies. Since every other body can be dispersed, torn apart, increased, decreased, decayed, and corrupted, the same would have to apply to that body if it were a body. These properties are incompatible with divinity because God is perfect and changeless. Therefore, the alleged divine body cannot exist; God is not a body.

Key Terms

تمزق (tearing)
زيادة (increase)
نقصان (decrease)
عفونة (decay)
فساد (corruption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God could be a body without being subject to dispersion or decay.
Correction: The proof shows that corporeality necessarily entails these properties based on the observable nature of all bodies.

Checkpoint

C) Dispersion, tearing, increase, decrease, decay, and corruption.
D) Only dispersion and tearing.

Key Takeaway
All bodies are subject to decay and corruption, so God—who is perfect—cannot be a body.

The Fifth Proof: Parts Require a Specifier
Argues that the parts of a body need a specifier, leading to a non‑corporeal maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equal nature of parts in a composite body necessitates a non-corporeal specifier, and why this argument is considered strong despite a philosophical objection.

Core Idea
Equal parts demand a specifier; that specifier cannot be a body.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the text, which argues that the arrangement of parts in any composite body requires a specifier that cannot itself be a body. It uses the equality of parts to show that without a specifying cause, any arrangement would be equally possible, so the actual arrangement points to a non-corporeal maker. The passage also notes a consequence regarding the celestial sphere that some philosophers reject.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Understand the equality of parts
Identify the key premise: all parts in the aggregate are equal in essence. This means there is no inherent difference between parts.
Equality in essence is the foundation of the argument.
3
Grasp the need for a specifier
Since parts are equal, any part could be anywhere. The actual arrangement (some inside, some outside) therefore requires a cause that specifies this arrangement—a specifier.
This step is the heart of the proof.
4
See the implication of non-corporeality
If the specifier were a body, it would itself be composite and need another specifier, leading to infinite regress. Hence the ultimate specifier must be non-corporeal.
This establishes God's transcendence.
5
Note the objection about the celestial sphere
The author acknowledges that this proof implies the celestial sphere could be torn and mended, which philosophers deny. However, the proof is still considered strong.
This note does not undermine the argument; it addresses a secondary consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس : إن الأجزاء المفترضة في ذلك المجموع تكون متساوية في تمام الماهية. ولا شك أن بعض تلك الأجزاء وقع في العمق ، وبعضها في السطح وكل ما صح على الشيء صح على مثله ، فالذي وقع في العمق يمكن أن يقع في السطح وبالعكس . وإن كان الأمر كذلك، كان وقوع كل جزء على الوجه الذي وقع عليه ، لا بد وأن يكون بتخصيص مخصص ، ويجعل جاعل . وذلك على إله العالم : محال . (۱) من (و). واعلم : أن هذه الحجة قوية . إلا أنها توجب صحة الخرق والالتئام على الفلك . والفلاسفة لا يقولون به .
And the fifth: The parts assumed in that aggregate are equal in complete essence. There is no doubt that some of those parts are in the interior and some on the surface, and whatever is true of a thing is true of its like, so what is in the interior can be on the surface and vice versa. If that is the case, then the occurrence of each part in the manner it occurs must be by the specification of a specifier and the making of a maker. And that with respect to the God of the world is impossible. (1) From (W). And know: This proof is strong, except that it necessitates the validity of tearing and mending for the celestial sphere, and the philosophers do not affirm that.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This fifth proof examines a composite body (the aggregate). It notes that its constituent parts are essentially equal. Some parts happen to be in the interior and others on the surface, but since they are similar, any part could occupy any position. The actual arrangement therefore requires a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) and a maker (jāʿil). If that maker itself were a body, the same problem recurs. Therefore, the ultimate maker must be non‑corporeal. The passage adds a note: this argument is strong, but it implies that the celestial sphere (falak) could be torn and mended—something philosophers reject. The argument is given nonetheless as a proof of God's non‑corporeality.

Key Terms
أجزاء (parts)
ماهية (essence)
تخصيص (specification)
مخصص (specifier)
جاعل (maker)
خرق (tearing)
التئام (mending)
فلك (celestial sphere)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The note about the falak being tearable shows the proof is flawed or invalid.
Correction: The note only points out a consequence that some philosophers reject; the proof itself remains strong and the author presents it anyway.

Checkpoint

B) Because parts are equal and any arrangement is possible, so a specific arrangement needs a cause.
C) Because parts are eternal.
D) Because the body is divine.

Key Takeaway
The equal nature of parts in a composite body forces us to posit a non-corporeal specifier, establishing God's transcendence beyond physical composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Divine Transcendence
