First Branch: Body as a Genus
First branch: body as genus leads to composition and contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the first branch of the fourth proof shows that if God were a body differing in constituents, He would be composite, implying contingency.

Core Idea
Differing in constituents makes body a genus; composition implies contingency.

This lesson examines the first logical branch of the fourth proof. If God were a body and differed from other bodies in some essential constituent, then 'body' becomes a genus, and God would be composed of that genus and a differentia. Every composite is possible, so God would be contingent — contradicting His necessary existence. This branch is therefore false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first branch
Read the Arabic and the translation for the first part of the proof, which ends with 'هذا خلف' . Pay attention to the logical structure: two suppositions are introduced, and the first is evaluated.
Notice that the author presents two possibilities before rejecting the first.
2
Identify the components
Identify the genus (body), the differentia (distinguishing constituent), and see how composition arises. Understand that the conclusion 'every composite is possible' is a known premise in kalam.
The argument relies on the axiom that composites are contingent because they require their parts to exist first.
3
Reflect on the contradiction
Why does composition contradict necessary existence? Because a necessary being cannot depend on its parts. Consider that if God were composite, He would need His parts to exist, making Him dependent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ، في كونه متحيزاً . وإما أن يخالفها بعد ذلك في شيء من المقومات ، وإما ألا يكون كذلك وعلى التقدير الأول يكون المتحيز جنساً تحته أنواع وعلى التقدير الثاني يكون نوعاً تحته أشخاص ونقول : الأول باطل . وإلا لكان واجب الوجود ، مركبا من الجنس ، وهو المتحيز ، ومن الفصل وهو المقوم الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، وكل مركب ممكن ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ، ممكن الوجود لذاته . هذا خلف . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، وهو أن يكون المتحيز نوعاً تحته أشخاص ، وذلك لأن المفهوم من المتحيز قدر مشترك بين كل الأشخاص وتعين كل واحد منها غير مشترك بينه وبين الأشخاص، فتعين كل واحد منها زائد على طبيعته النوعية ، والمقتضى لذلك التعين المعين إن كان هو تلك الماهية ، أو شيء من لوازمها ، وجب أن يكون ذلك النوع مخصوصاً بذلك الشخص ، لكنا فرضناه مشتركاً فيه بين الأشخاص . هذا خلف
The fourth proof: If He were a body, He would be equal to other bodies in being a body. Then He would either differ from them in something among the constituents, or not. On the first supposition, 'body' would be a genus with species under it. On the second supposition, it would be a species with individuals under it. We say: The first is false; otherwise the Necessary Existence would be composed of the genus (which is body) and the differentia (which is the constituent by which it is distinguished from others). And every composite is possible. So the Necessary Existence in itself would be possible in itself, which is contradictory. The second is also false, namely that body is a species under it individuals. This is because the concept of body is a common meaning among all individuals, and the particularization of each individual is not common. So the particularization of each is additional to its specific nature. If the cause of that particularization is that essence or something from its concomitants, then that species would be specific to that individual, but we assumed it is common among individuals. This is contradictory.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This fourth proof uses an argument from genus and species. It supposes God is a body. Then either He differs from other bodies in some essential constituent (qawwām) or He does not. If He differs, then 'body' becomes a genus and God is a species under it, meaning God is composed of a genus (body) and a differentia—thus composite; every composite is possible (contingent), so God would be possible, not necessary—a contradiction. If He does not differ, then all bodies are the same species with individuals. But then each individual would have a particularization beyond the shared species nature, which requires a cause. If that cause is the essence or its concomitant, the species would be unique to that individual, contrary to the assumption that it is common. Thus both possibilities lead to contradiction, proving God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جنس (genus)
فصل (differentia)
مقوم (constituent)
تركيب (composition)
مركب (composite)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that being composite does not affect necessity, or that a composite can be necessary if its parts are necessary.
Correction: The proof states that every composite is possible (contingent) because it depends on its parts. A necessary being requires no external cause, and parts would be causal components. Therefore a necessary being cannot be composite.

Checkpoint
What does the first branch conclude about the supposition that God is a body and differs from other bodies in a constituent?
A) It is impossible because it leads to composition and contingency.
B) It is possible because composition does not imply contingency.
C) It is necessary because only then can God be a body.
D) It is false because bodies cannot be genera.

Key Takeaway
If God were a body with a different constituent, He would be composite and thus contingent, contradicting His necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined Allah as having parts or attributes that are separate from His essence? How does this proof expose that mental image as a contradiction to His necessity? What will you do today to correct that image in your heart?
?
If everything composite is contingent, then your own existence depends on Allah completely. Does your daily life reflect that dependence, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What one change can you make today to live in awareness of your contingency?
?
This proof shows that the Necessary Existence cannot be composed. Is there any area of your ‘aqeedah where you have accepted a notion of Allah that includes composition, even subtly? Pray for guidance to purify your belief and take a specific step to learn more about divine simplicity.

Second Branch: Body as a Species
Second branch: body as species with individuals leads to particularization problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second branch of the fourth proof shows that if God were a body without differing in constituents, particularization would lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Without differing, body is a species; particularization requires a cause beyond essence.

This lesson covers the second logical branch. If God does not differ from other bodies in any essential constituent, then all bodies share the same species nature. But then each individual body has a particularization (a specific existence) that is not part of the common species concept. This particularization must have a cause. If that cause is the essence itself, then the species would be unique to one individual, contradicting its commonality. Thus the second branch also fails, proving God cannot be a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second branch
Read the Arabic and translation for the second part, starting from 'والثاني أيضاً باطل' . Notice how the argument uses the concept of particularization (تعين).
This branch deals with the second possibility: God does not differ in constituents.
2
Understand particularization
Recognize that each individual body has a specific existence (particularization) that is not included in the common species concept. This extra factor requires a cause.
The argument says if the cause is the essence or its concomitants, then the species would be specific to one individual.
3
See the contradiction
If the cause of particularization is the essence itself, then that essence would belong only to that specific individual, not common to all. The species was assumed common; thus contradiction.
This shows that the second possibility also leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ، في كونه متحيزاً . وإما أن يخالفها بعد ذلك في شيء من المقومات ، وإما ألا يكون كذلك وعلى التقدير الأول يكون المتحيز جنساً تحته أنواع وعلى التقدير الثاني يكون نوعاً تحته أشخاص ونقول : الأول باطل . وإلا لكان واجب الوجود ، مركبا من الجنس ، وهو المتحيز ، ومن الفصل وهو المقوم الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، وكل مركب ممكن ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ، ممكن الوجود لذاته . هذا خلف . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، وهو أن يكون المتحيز نوعاً تحته أشخاص ، وذلك لأن المفهوم من المتحيز قدر مشترك بين كل الأشخاص وتعين كل واحد منها غير مشترك بينه وبين الأشخاص، فتعين كل واحد منها زائد على طبيعته النوعية ، والمقتضى لذلك التعين المعين إن كان هو تلك الماهية ، أو شيء من لوازمها ، وجب أن يكون ذلك النوع مخصوصاً بذلك الشخص ، لكنا فرضناه مشتركاً فيه بين الأشخاص . هذا خلف
The fourth proof: If He were a body, He would be equal to other bodies in being a body. Then He would either differ from them in something among the constituents, or not. On the first supposition, 'body' would be a genus with species under it. On the second supposition, it would be a species with individuals under it. We say: The first is false; otherwise the Necessary Existence would be composed of the genus (which is body) and the differentia (which is the constituent by which it is distinguished from others). And every composite is possible. So the Necessary Existence in itself would be possible in itself, which is contradictory. The second is also false, namely that body is a species under it individuals. This is because the concept of body is a common meaning among all individuals, and the particularization of each individual is not common. So the particularization of each is additional to its specific nature. If the cause of that particularization is that essence or something from its concomitants, then that species would be specific to that individual, but we assumed it is common among individuals. This is contradictory.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This fourth proof uses an argument from genus and species. It supposes God is a body. Then either He differs from other bodies in some essential constituent (qawwām) or He does not. If He differs, then 'body' becomes a genus and God is a species under it, meaning God is composed of a genus (body) and a differentia—thus composite; every composite is possible (contingent), so God would be possible, not necessary—a contradiction. If He does not differ, then all bodies are the same species with individuals. But then each individual would have a particularization beyond the shared species nature, which requires a cause. If that cause is the essence or its concomitant, the species would be unique to that individual, contrary to the assumption that it is common. Thus both possibilities lead to contradiction, proving God cannot be a body.

Key Terms
نوع (species)
تعين (particularization)
ماهية (essence)
لازم (concomitant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that particularization of individuals does not require a cause, or that it can be explained by the essence itself without contradicting commonality.
Correction: The argument shows that if the essence causes particularization, then that essence would belong only to that individual, undermining the commonality of the species. Thus particularization must come from an external cause, which means bodies are contingent.

Checkpoint
Why does the second branch conclude that the supposition is false?
A) Because particularization cannot be caused by the essence without contradicting commonality.
B) Because bodies cannot be species.
C) Because composition implies contingency.
D) Because the first branch was already false.

Key Takeaway
Even if God were a body without differing in constituents, the need for individual particularization leads to contradiction.

The Third Proof: Extendedness Implies Finitude and Contingency
Proof that extendedness entails finitude and contingency, ruling out extension for the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that if God were extended, He would be finite and thus contingent, which contradicts His necessity.

Core Idea
Extension leads to finitude, finitude to contingency—and God is above all three.

This passage presents a crisp syllogism for a core tenet of Islamic theology: that Allah is not subject to spatial extension. The author builds the argument from two rationally necessary premises. Let's follow the logical steps and see why extendedness is incompatible with being the Necessary of Existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: two premises lead to a conclusion.
The passage is concise; every sentence plays a role.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the two premises: (1) every extended is finite, (2) every finite is contingent. Note the reasons given for each.
The first is supported by proofs of finitude of dimensions; the second by the possibility of conceiving greater or lesser magnitude.
3
Understand 'necessary knowledge'
The author says knowledge of this possibility (imkān) is necessary (ḍarūrī). Reflect on why it is not empirical but a rational necessity.
This shows the argument is a priori.
4
Follow the conclusion
See how the conclusion follows: no extended thing is the Necessary Being, and the Necessary Being is not extended.
It's a universal negative, both ways.
5
Reflect on the doctrine
Ask yourself: what does this proof teach us about the nature of Allah? It upholds divine transcendence beyond spatial attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان متناهياً ، وكل متناء ممكن . وواجب الوجود ليس بممكن ، فالمتحيز لا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته . أما بيان أن كل متحيز فهو متناه ، فللدلائل الدالة على تناهي الأبعاد ، وأما أن كل متناه ممكن ، فلان كل مقدار فإنه يمكن فرض كونه أزيد منه قدراً ، وأنقص منه قدراً . والعلم بثبوت هذا الإمكان ضروري - فيثبت : أن كل متحيز ممكن ، ويثبت أن واجب الوجود ليس بممكن ، ينتج : فلا شيء من المتحيزات بواجب الوجود ، وينعكس فلا شيء من واجب الوجود بمتحيز
The third argument: If He were extended (mutahayyiz), He would be finite (mutanāh), and every finite [thing] is contingent (mumkin). And the Necessary of Existence (wājib al-wujūd) is not possible (mumkin), so the extended cannot be Necessary of Existence in itself. As for the demonstration that every extended is finite, it is by the proofs indicating the finitude of dimensions. And as for that every finite is contingent, it is because for every magnitude (miqdār) it is possible to suppose it greater in amount and lesser in amount. And the knowledge of the establishment of this possibility (imkān) is necessary (ḍarūrī). Thus it is established that every extended is contingent, and it is established that the Necessary of Existence is not contingent, producing: none of the extended things is the Necessary of Existence, and conversely: none of the Necessary of Existence is extended.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against God being extended (mutahayyiz). The argument proceeds syllogistically: (1) If God were extended, He would be finite; (2) every finite thing is contingent; (3) the Necessary of Existence is not contingent; so God is not extended. The author explains the two premises: that every extended is finite is proven by established proofs of the finitude of dimensions; that every finite is contingent is shown by the fact that for any magnitude one can conceive a greater or lesser, which is a necessary judgment of reason. The conclusion is the universal negative: no extended thing is the Necessary of Existence, and vice versa.

Key Terms
متحيز
متناه
ممكن
واجب الوجود
أبعاد
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof depends on empirical observation that the universe is finite.
Correction: The author states that the possibility of conceiving greater or lesser magnitudes is a necessary rational judgment, not an empirical finding.

Checkpoint
Why is every finite thing contingent according to the argument?
A) Because it is composed of parts.
B) Because it is possible to suppose it greater or lesser.
C) Because it requires a cause.
D) Because it is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary of Existence cannot be extended because extendedness implies finitude and contingency, while necessity excludes contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just reasoned that God cannot be extended—He is not limited by space or size. Is there an area of your life where you behave as if God is limited, as if His power or care cannot reach a certain situation? What would change if you truly believed He is free from all limitations right now?
?
The proof shows that for any finite thing, you can imagine it larger or smaller. Consider your own life—you are finite and contingent. Does this realization humble you? If you are contingent, then your entire existence depends on the Necessary Being every moment. How would your daily actions change if you lived in constant awareness of that dependence?
?
This lesson argues that the Necessary of Existence cannot be extended. Yet many people imagine God in human terms—as having a place, a size, or a form. Ask yourself honestly: Have you ever pictured God as somehow located 'up there' or as having a shape? What would it mean for your worship to know that He is beyond all extension—neither here nor there, but the very ground of existence?

God Cannot Be Extended: The Genus-Differentia Argument
Proof that differing by a constitutive implies composition and contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if God were extended and differed from other extended things by an additional constitutive, He would be composite and therefore contingent.

Core Idea
If God were extended, He would be composite of genus and differentia — every composite is contingent.

This lesson unpacks the first branch of the fourth proof: if God shares extendedness with all extended things but differs by a constitutive (muqawwim), then He becomes composite of genus (extendedness) and differentia (that constitutive). The author shows this leads to an impossibility: the Necessary of Existence would be contingent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: hypothesis, division into two possibilities, then refutation of the first.
The first possibility is that God differs from other extended things by a constitutive.
2
Identify the first possibility
The passage says: if God differs by a constitutive, then extendedness becomes a genus and the constitutive becomes a differentia. This makes God composite.
Genus + differentia = species; here God would be a species, thus composite.
3
Understand the contradiction
Every composite is contingent (mumkin). If God were composite, He would be contingent, contradicting His necessary existence.
The author states: 'كل مركب ممكن' (every composite is contingent).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
This branch is false because it leads to a contradiction. Therefore, the first possibility cannot hold for the Necessary of Existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ، في كونه متحيزاً . وإما أن يخالفها بعد ذلك في شيء من المقومات ، وإما ألا يكون كذلك وعلى التقدير الأول يكون المتحيز جنساً تحته أنواع وعلى التقدير الثاني يكون نوعاً تحته أشخاص ونقول : الأول باطل . وإلا لكان واجب الوجود ، مركبا من الجنس ، وهو المتحيز ، ومن الفصل وهو المقوم الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، وكل مركب ممكن ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ، ممكن الوجود لذاته . هذا خلف . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، وهو أن يكون المتحيز نوعاً تحته أشخاص ، وذلك لأن المفهوم من المتحيز قدر مشترك بين كل الأشخاص وتعين كل واحد منها غير مشترك بينه وبين الأشخاص، فتعين كل واحد منها زائد على طبيعته النوعية ، والمقتضى لذلك التعين المعين إن كان هو تلك الماهية ، أو شيء من لوازمها ، وجب أن يكون ذلك النوع مخصوصاً بذلك الشخص ، لكنا فرضناه مشتركاً فيه بين الأشخاص . هذا خلف وإن كان أمراً منفصلا، فكل شخص من أشخاص الجسم المتحيز ، إنما يتعين بسبب منفصل . فلا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته فثبت : أن كل جسم فهو ممكن لذاته، وما لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته ، امتنع أن يكون جسماً .
The fourth argument: If He were extended, He would be equal to other extended things in being extended. Then either He differs from them afterwards in some of the constitutives (muqawwimāt) or He does not differ. On the first supposition, the extended (mutahayyiz) would be a genus (jins) under which are species (anwā‘). On the second supposition, it would be a species under which are individuals (ashkhāṣ). And we say: the first is false, for then the Necessary of Existence would be composed of the genus, which is extendedness, and the differentia (faṣl), which is the constitutive by which He is distinguished from others. And every composite (murakkab) is contingent (mumkin), so the Necessary of Existence in itself would be contingent in itself – this is a contradiction. The second is also false, namely that the extended is a species under which are individuals, because the concept of extended is a common share (qadr mushtarak) among all individuals, and the individuation (ta‘ayyun) of each one is not shared between it and the individuals. So the individuation of each one is additional to its specific nature. The determinant (muqtaḍī) for that specific individuation: if it is that essence (māhiyya) or something from its necessary concomitants (lawāzim), then that species would have to be particular to that individual, but we supposed it shared among individuals – this is a contradiction. And if it is a separate matter (amr munfaṣil), then each individual of extended body individuates due to a separate cause, so it would not be Necessary of Existence in itself. Thus it is established that every body is contingent in itself, and whatever is not possible of existence in itself is impossible to be a body.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which is a more complex argument based on the classification of genera and species. The author begins by stating that if God were extended, He would share the property of extendedness with all other extended things. Then two possibilities arise: either God differs from other extended things by some additional constitutive (muqawwim), or He does not. If He does, then extendedness becomes a genus and the additional constitutive a differentia, making God composite of genus and differentia. Every composite is contingent, so God would be contingent, a contradiction. If He does not differ, then extendedness would be a species under which there are individuals. The author then examines the notion of a species with individuals. The concept of 'extended' is a common meaning shared by all individuals, but each individual has a specific individuation (ta‘ayyun) that is not shared. This individuation must be caused either by the essence itself or by a separate cause. If by the essence, that species would be unique to that individual, contradicting the assumption of multiple individuals. If by a separate cause, then the individual depends on an external cause, making it contingent. Thus, in both cases, the Necessary of Existence cannot be extended. The conclusion is that every body is contingent, and what is not contingent cannot be a body.

Key Terms
متحيز
جنس
نوع
مقوم
فصل
مركب
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if God is composite, He could still be necessary if the parts are necessary.
Correction: The passage shows that composition inherently implies contingency, regardless of the nature of the parts.

Checkpoint
What does the first branch of the argument conclude?
A) God differs from other extended things by a constitutive.
B) If God were extended and differed by a constitutive, He would be composite and thus impossible for the Necessary of Existence.
C) Extendedness is a genus under which there are species.
D) The second possibility is also false.

Key Takeaway
If God were extended and differed from other extended things by a constitutive, He would be composite and therefore contingent, contradicting His necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument concludes that if God were extended, He would be composite and thus contingent. Does your heart truly accept that God is absolutely simple and beyond any physical extension, or do you still unconsciously conceive of Him as having some form? What step will you take today to purify your 'aqeedah of any lingering anthropomorphism?
?
Every composite is contingent—that means your existence depends on your parts. How does the realization that God, unlike you, is not composite and not dependent on anything change the way you approach Him in your prayers? Are you willing to examine your own concept of God and remove any subtle assumptions that He could be limited by space or direction?

God Cannot Be Extended: The Species-Individual Argument
Proof that extendedness as a species leads to contingency via individuation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if God were extended without any differentiating constitutive, He would still be contingent due to the problem of individuation.

Core Idea
Extendedness as a species implies individuation by an external cause, making it contingent.

This lesson examines the second branch of the fourth proof: if God does not differ from other extended things by any constitutive, then extendedness would be a species with multiple individuals. The author then shows that the individuation (ta‘ayyun) of each individual must come either from the essence itself or from a separate cause — both lead to contingency, so the Necessary of Existence cannot be extended.

Guided Path
1
Read the second branch passage
Read the Arabic from 'والثاني أيضاً باطل' to the end. Follow the argument about the concept of 'extended' as a common share and individuation.
This branch assumes no constitutive difference.
2
Understand the concept of 'extended' as a species
The passage states that 'extended' is a common meaning (qadr mushtarak) shared by all individuals. Each individual has a specific individuation (ta‘ayyun) not shared.
The individuation is additional to the species nature.
3
Examine the two possibilities for the cause of individuation
The cause of individuation could be the essence itself (or its necessary concomitants) or a separate external cause. Both lead to problems.
4
Trace the first sub-case: essence causes individuation
If the essence causes individuation, then that species would be unique to that individual, contradicting the assumption that the species has multiple individuals.
This is a contradiction.
5
Trace the second sub-case: separate cause
If a separate cause individuates, then the individual depends on an external cause, making it contingent. So the individual cannot be necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان متحيزاً لكان مساوياً لسائر المتحيزات ، في كونه متحيزاً . وإما أن يخالفها بعد ذلك في شيء من المقومات ، وإما ألا يكون كذلك وعلى التقدير الأول يكون المتحيز جنساً تحته أنواع وعلى التقدير الثاني يكون نوعاً تحته أشخاص ونقول : الأول باطل . وإلا لكان واجب الوجود ، مركبا من الجنس ، وهو المتحيز ، ومن الفصل وهو المقوم الذي به يمتاز عن غيره ، وكل مركب ممكن ، فواجب الوجود لذاته ، ممكن الوجود لذاته . هذا خلف . والثاني أيضاً باطل ، وهو أن يكون المتحيز نوعاً تحته أشخاص ، وذلك لأن المفهوم من المتحيز قدر مشترك بين كل الأشخاص وتعين كل واحد منها غير مشترك بينه وبين الأشخاص، فتعين كل واحد منها زائد على طبيعته النوعية ، والمقتضى لذلك التعين المعين إن كان هو تلك الماهية ، أو شيء من لوازمها ، وجب أن يكون ذلك النوع مخصوصاً بذلك الشخص ، لكنا فرضناه مشتركاً فيه بين الأشخاص . هذا خلف وإن كان أمراً منفصلا، فكل شخص من أشخاص الجسم المتحيز ، إنما يتعين بسبب منفصل . فلا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته فثبت : أن كل جسم فهو ممكن لذاته، وما لا يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته ، امتنع أن يكون جسماً .
The fourth argument: If He were extended, He would be equal to other extended things in being extended. Then either He differs from them afterwards in some of the constitutives (muqawwimāt) or He does not differ. On the first supposition, the extended (mutahayyiz) would be a genus (jins) under which are species (anwā‘). On the second supposition, it would be a species under which are individuals (ashkhāṣ). And we say: the first is false, for then the Necessary of Existence would be composed of the genus, which is extendedness, and the differentia (faṣl), which is the constitutive by which He is distinguished from others. And every composite (murakkab) is contingent (mumkin), so the Necessary of Existence in itself would be contingent in itself – this is a contradiction. The second is also false, namely that the extended is a species under which are individuals, because the concept of extended is a common share (qadr mushtarak) among all individuals, and the individuation (ta‘ayyun) of each one is not shared between it and the individuals. So the individuation of each one is additional to its specific nature. The determinant (muqtaḍī) for that specific individuation: if it is that essence (māhiyya) or something from its necessary concomitants (lawāzim), then that species would have to be particular to that individual, but we supposed it shared among individuals – this is a contradiction. And if it is a separate matter (amr munfaṣil), then each individual of extended body individuates due to a separate cause, so it would not be Necessary of Existence in itself. Thus it is established that every body is contingent in itself, and whatever is not possible of existence in itself is impossible to be a body.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which is a more complex argument based on the classification of genera and species. The author begins by stating that if God were extended, He would share the property of extendedness with all other extended things. Then two possibilities arise: either God differs from other extended things by some additional constitutive (muqawwim), or He does not. If He does, then extendedness becomes a genus and the additional constitutive a differentia, making God composite of genus and differentia. Every composite is contingent, so God would be contingent, a contradiction. If He does not differ, then extendedness would be a species under which there are individuals. The author then examines the notion of a species with individuals. The concept of 'extended' is a common meaning shared by all individuals, but each individual has a specific individuation (ta‘ayyun) that is not shared. This individuation must be caused either by the essence itself or by a separate cause. If by the essence, that species would be unique to that individual, contradicting the assumption of multiple individuals. If by a separate cause, then the individual depends on an external cause, making it contingent. Thus, in both cases, the Necessary of Existence cannot be extended. The conclusion is that every body is contingent, and what is not contingent cannot be a body.

Key Terms
نوع
شخص
تعين
قدر مشترك
لوازم
ماهية
أمر منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if God is extended but not composite (i.e., as a simple species), He could still be necessary.
Correction: The passage shows that even as a species, the problem of individuation forces either uniqueness or dependence, both leading to contingency.

Checkpoint
What happens if the cause of individuation is the essence itself?
A) The species would have multiple individuals.
B) The species would be particular to that individual, contradicting the assumption.
C) The individual would be contingent due to external cause.
D) The individual would be necessary.

Key Takeaway
Even if God were extended without a differentiating constitutive, the need for individuation forces contingency, so God cannot be extended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every extended thing is contingent and depends on an external cause for its individuation, what does that say about the physical objects you rely on for your identity and security? Are you placing your trust in something that is inherently dependent and passing?
?
This argument shows that God cannot be a body because bodies are contingent. Have you ever unconsciously imagined God as having physical attributes like size or location? How does it feel to realize that any such conception is a limitation on the Necessary of Existence?
?
The proof forces you to acknowledge that nothing extended is self-sufficient. How does this truth challenge your tendency to seek ultimate satisfaction in the material world? What step can you take today to shift your ultimate reliance to the Non-Extended alone?

Fifth Proof: If God Were a Body, He Would Be Subject to Division
Proof that if God were a body, He would be subject to division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts, leading to the possibility of division, which contradicts His necessity and self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
A body implies composition; composition implies contingency; so God cannot be a body.

This lesson presents the fifth proof from the classical kalam tradition that God cannot be a body. The argument starts with a conditional: if God were a body, then He would be subject to division. Since division is impossible for the Necessary Existence, God cannot be a body. We will walk through the detailed reasoning, analyzing how composition leads to a need for an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'If He were a body, then division would be possible...'
The argument uses a proof by contradiction.
2
Identify the key components
Find the statement of the proof, the explanation of the entailment, the reasoning about parts and sides, and the conclusion about the separate cause.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace how composition leads to simple parts, then to equal sides, then to transfer of touching, and finally to the possibility of separation.
Each step relies on the previous one.
4
Understand the impossibility
Consider why the possibility of division makes the composite contingent on a separate cause, contradicting the self-sufficiency of the Necessary of Existence.
This is the crux of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كان جسماً ، الجاز عليه التفرق والتمزق ، وهذا محال . [ فذاك محال ] بيان الملازمة : أنه إذا كان مركباً من الأجزاء ، وجب انتهاء تحليل تلك الأجزاء إلى أجزاء يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه بسيطاً ، مبراً عن التركيب والتأليف ، وإذا كان [ كذلك كان ] طبع يمينه مساوياً . لطبع يساره ، وإلا قصار مركباً . وإذا ثبت مساواة الجانبين في الطبيعة والماهية ، فكل ما كان ممسوساً بجانب يمينه ، وجب أن يصح كونه ممسوساً بجانب يساره ، ضرورة أن كل ما صح على شيء ، فإنه يصح أيضاً على مثله ، وإذا كان كذلك ، فكما صح على ذلك الجزء ، أن يماس الجزء الثاني ، بأحد وجهيه ، وجب أن [ يصح أن ] يماسه بالوجه الثاني. وإذا ثبت جواز ذلك ، ثبت جواز صحة التفرق عليه والتمزق عليه. وإنما قلنا : إن ذلك محال ، لأنه لما صح الاجتماع والافتراق على تلك الأجزاء ، لم يترجح الاجتماع على الافتراق إلا بسبب منفصل ، فيلزم افتقاره في وجوده إلى السبب المنفصل . وواجب الوجود [ لذاته ، يمتنع أن يكون كذلك ، فيثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس جسماً .
The fifth argument: If He were a body (jism), then division (tafarruq) and tearing (tamazzuq) would be possible upon Him, and this is impossible. So that is impossible. Explanation of the entailment: Since if He is composed of parts, the analysis of those parts must end at parts each of which is in itself simple (basīṭ), free from composition and arrangement. And if that is so, the nature of its right side is equal to the nature of its left side, otherwise it would be composite. And if the equality of the two sides in nature and essence is established, then whatever is touched by its right side must be possible to be touched by its left side, necessarily that whatever is possible for a thing is also possible for its like. And if that is so, just as it is possible for that part to touch the second part with one of its faces, it must be possible that it touches it with the second face. And if the possibility of that is established, the possibility of division and tearing upon it is established. And we say: that is impossible, because when assembly (ijtimā‘) and separation (iftirāq) are possible for those parts, assembly would not be preferred over separation except by a separate cause (sabab munfaṣil), so it entails its need in its existence for the separate cause. And the Necessary of Existence in itself is impossible to be such. So it is established that the Necessary of Existence in itself is not a body.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof, which argues from the impossibility of division for the Necessary of Existence. The author states that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts, and division and tearing would be possible. But that is impossible for the Necessary. The explanation: any composite of parts can be analyzed down to simple parts. For a simple part, its right and left sides are identical in nature. Therefore, if one side touches another part, the other side should also be able to touch it, implying that the part can be separated from that other part by touching with a different side. Thus, the possibility of separation (and hence division) is established. But if separation is possible, then the composition is not necessary but contingent; it requires a separate cause to maintain the assembly. This need for a cause contradicts the self-sufficiency of the Necessary of Existence. Therefore, God cannot be a body.

تفرق

بسيط

جانب
مماسة
اجتماع
افتراق
سبب منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes God actually has parts and is therefore a body.
Correction: The argument does not assert that God has parts; it shows that if He were a body, He would have parts, which leads to impossibility. It is a hypothetical syllogism.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider division and tearing impossible for the Necessary of Existence?
A) Because bodies are simple and cannot be divided.
B) Because it would imply a need for a separate cause to maintain unity.
C) Because the Necessary of Existence is composed of multiple parts.
D) Because division is impossible for all bodies.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that bodiness entails composition, which entails contingency, so the Necessary Existence cannot be a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that any composite depends on something outside itself for its unity. What 'composite' areas of your life—your identity, your relationships, your accomplishments—do you treat as self-sufficient? How would acknowledging their dependence on Allah change your attachment to them?
?
If God cannot be divided, what does that mean for your divided heart? In what specific area are you trying to serve both Allah and something else, as if He could be compartmentalized? What step can you take today to reunite your focus on Him alone?
?
The argument says the Necessary Existence needs no cause. Yet you often act as if your worth or security depends on human approval or material success. What is one concrete attachment you can surrender this week to affirm that only Allah is self-sufficient? Make a commitment now.

Fifth Proof: God Cannot Be a Body Due to Possibility of Division
Sanusi presents the fifth proof that Wajib al-Wujud cannot be a body, based on divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the fifth proof that the Necessary Being cannot be a body, based on the possibility of division.

Core Idea
If God were a body, He would depend on an external cause; therefore He is not a body.

Sanusi presents a refined argument against corporeality of God. The proof shows that assuming God is a body leads to the possibility of division, which in turn implies dependence on an external cause—contradicting the very nature of the Necessary Being. This lesson walks through the steps of this reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the phrase 'بيان الملازمة' which introduces the reasoning.
2
Identify the entailment chain
Trace how Sanusi connects the assumption of being a body to the possibility of division via the composition of parts and equality of sides.
The key link is that simple parts have equal sides, allowing symmetric touching and thus divisibility.
3
Grasp the impossibility
Understand why the possibility of division leads to the need for an external cause, and why that is impossible for the Necessary Being.
The conclusion is that Wajib al-Wujud is not a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة: لو كان جسماً، لجاز عليه التفرق والتمزق، وهذا محال. بيان الملازمة: أنه إذا كان مركباً من الأجزاء، وجب انتهاء تحليل تلك الأجزاء إلى أجزاء يكون كل واحد منها في نفسه بسيطاً، مبراً عن التركيب والتأليف، وإذا كان كذلك كان طبع يمينه مساوياً لطبع يساره، وإلا لكان مركباً. وإذا ثبت مساواة الجانبين في الطبيعة والماهية، فكل ما كان ممسوساً بجانب يمينه، وجب أن يصح كونه ممسوساً بجانب يساره، ضرورة أن كل ما صح على شيء، فإنه يصح أيضاً على مثله. وإذا كان كذلك، فكما صح على ذلك الجزء أن يماس الجزء الثاني بأحد وجهيه، وجب أن يصح أن يماسه بالوجه الثاني. وإذا ثبت جواز ذلك، ثبت جواز صحة التفرق والتمزق عليه. وإنما قلنا: إن ذلك محال، لأنه لما صح الاجتماع والافتراق على تلك الأجزاء، لم يترجح الاجتماع على الافتراق إلا بسبب منفصل، فيلزم افتقاره في وجوده إلى السبب المنفصل. وواجب الوجود لذاته يمتنع أن يكون كذلك، فيثبت أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس جسماً.
The fifth proof: If He were a body, then division and fragmentation would be possible for Him, and this is impossible. Explanation of the entailment: When He is composed of parts, the analysis of those parts must end at parts each of which is simple in itself, free from composition and arrangement. And if that is the case, then the character of its right side is equal to the character of its left side; otherwise it would be composite. And if the equality of the two sides in nature and essence is established, then everything that is touched on its right side must be possible to be touched on its left side, because everything that is possible for a thing is also possible for its like. And if that is the case, then just as it is possible for that part to touch the second part with one of its two faces, it must be possible for it to touch it with the second face. And if the possibility of that is established, then the possibility of division and fragmentation upon it is established. And we say: That is impossible, because when combination and separation are possible for those parts, combination over separation is not preponderated except by an external cause, necessitating its dependence for its existence on an external cause. And the Necessary Being in essence cannot be like that; thus it is established that the Necessary Being in essence is not a body.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof that God (Wajib al-Wujud) is not a body. It is a proof by reductio ad absurdum. The argument proceeds in several steps: First, assume God is a body; then He would be composed of parts, and analysis of those parts must lead to simple, non-composite parts. These simple parts will have equal natural properties on all sides (since they are simple). If a part can be touched on one side, it must also be possible to be touched on the other equal side. This allows the possibility of separation and division of those parts, meaning the composite could break apart. However, for a composite to exist as a combination, its parts must be held together; if separation is equally possible, then the combination requires an external cause to prefer combination over separation. That would make the being dependent on an external cause, contradicting the self-sufficiency of the Necessary Being. Therefore, God cannot be a body.

تفرق

الملازمة
بسيط
السبب المنفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that actual division occurs, but it only establishes possibility.
Correction: The proof does not require actual division; the mere possibility of division is enough to show dependence on an external cause for combination, which contradicts necessary existence.

Checkpoint
Why does the possibility of division lead to the need for an external cause?
A) Because God cannot be divided.
B) Because if parts can separate, their combination must be chosen by something else.
C) Because division is impossible for bodies.
D) Because simple parts are indivisible.

Key Takeaway
The fifth proof demonstrates that if God were a body, He would be composite and thus dependent on an external cause for His parts to stay together, which contradicts His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This proof shows that God is not a body, meaning He is not confined by space or time. Does your daily life reflect a belief in a God who is beyond your physical surroundings? In what ways do you act as if God is limited or absent?
?
The argument uses logic to prove God's transcendence. Are you willing to submit your intellect to the truth of revelation and reason, even when it challenges your inclinations? What attachments make you want to reduce God to a manageable concept?

Sixth Proof: God Cannot Be a Body
Sanusi argues that God cannot be a body through a tripartite impossibility analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing divine attributes to a composite body leads to logical absurdities, and how the objection from human composition is answered.

Core Idea
A composite body cannot house divine attributes without implying multiplicity of gods.

Sanusi presents the sixth proof that God is not a body, using a rigorous reduction to absurdity. He shows that if God were a body, then the location of divine attributes within that body would lead to either an atomic god, an indivisible attribute, or multiple gods. The objection from human bodies is then distinguished, reinforcing God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening lines: if God were spatially extended, He would be a body composed of parts. Note that no rational person says He is an indivisible atom.
This sets up the dilemma.
2
Analyze the three possibilities
Identify the three ways divine attributes could relate to the parts: either a single attribute resides in all parts, or it is divided among parts, or each part has its own separate attributes.
3
See how each is refuted
Sanusi rejects each: first, a single attribute cannot subsist in multiple loci; second, knowledge is indivisible; third, separate attributes in each part imply multiple gods.
4
Consider the objection from humanity
An objector points out that human bodies are also composite yet we attribute knowledge to a single person. Understand the two answers: philosophers attribute to soul; Ash'aris accept each part is knowing but that doesn't imply multiple gods.
The distinction rests on the impossibility of multiple gods in God's case.
5
Grasp the conclusion
God's oneness and transcendence preclude any composition that would necessitate multiple divine attributes or multiple gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة: لو كان متحيزاً لكان جسماً، لأنه لم يقل أحد من العقلاء بأنه في حجم الجوهر الفرد، وإذا كان جسماً كان مركباً من الأجزاء، فإما أن يكون الموصوف بالعلم والقدرة والصفات المعتبرة في الإلهية: جزءاً واحداً من تلك الأجزاء، وإما أن يكون الموصوف بتلك الصفات مجموع تلك الأجزاء. فإن كان الأول كان الإله هو ذلك الجزء الواحد، منفرداً، فيعود الأمر إلى ما ذكرناه من أن الإله يكون في حجم الجوهر الفرد، وإن كان الثاني فنقول: إما أن تقوم الصفة الواحدة بجميع تلك الأجزاء، وإما أن تتوزع أجزاء تلك الصفة على تلك الأجزاء، وإما أن يقوم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علم على حدة وقدرة على حدة. والأول باطل لأن قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمحال الكثيرة غير معقول. والثاني محال لأن كون العلم قابلاً للقسمة محال على ما بيناه في مسألة إثبات النفس. والثالث أيضاً محال لأنه يلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء موصوفاً بجملة الصفات المعتبرة في الإلهية، وذلك يوجب تعدد الآلهة، وذلك محال. فإن قيل: ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في الإنسان، فإن مجموع بدنه لا شك أنه مركب من الأجزاء الكثيرة، فيلزم أن يقوم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علم على حدة وقدرة على حدة، فيلزم أن يكون الإنسان الواحد علماء قادرين كثيرين، وذلك باطل. قلنا: أما الفلاسفة فقد طردوا قولهم في الكل وزعموا: أن الموصوف بالعلم والقدرة هو النفس لا الجسم، وإلا لزم هذا المحال. وأما الأشعري فإنه التزم كون كل واحد من أجزاء الإنسان عالماً قادراً، وذلك في غاية البعد، إلا أن التزامه وإن كان بعيداً، لكن لا يلزم منه محال. أما التزام ذلك في حق الله تعالى فهو محال لأنه يوجب القول بتعدد الآلهة وهو محال.
The sixth proof: If He were spatially extended, He would be a body, because no rational person says that He is the size of an indivisible atom. And if He were a body, He would be composed of parts. Then either the one described by knowledge, power, and the attributes considered in divinity is a single one of those parts, or the described by those attributes is the totality of those parts. If the first, then the god is that single part, alone, and the matter returns to what we mentioned: that the god is the size of an indivisible atom. If the second, we say: either the single attribute subsists in all those parts, or the parts of that attribute are distributed over those parts, or in each of those parts there subsists a separate knowledge and a separate power. The first is invalid because the subsistence of a single attribute in multiple loci is inconceivable. The second is invalid because knowledge being divisible is impossible, as we explained in the question of establishing the soul. The third is also invalid because it necessitates that each of those parts be described by the totality of attributes considered in divinity, and that necessitates a multiplicity of gods, and that is impossible. If it is said: The proof you have presented applies to the human being, for the sum of his body is undoubtedly composed of many parts, so it would necessitate that in each of those parts there subsist a separate knowledge and separate power, necessitating that a single human being be many knowers, many powerful beings, and that is invalid. We say: As for the philosophers, they applied their statement consistently and claimed that the described by knowledge and power is the soul, not the body; otherwise this absurdity would follow. As for al-Ash‛ari, he adhered to the position that each of the human's parts is knowing and powerful, and that is extremely far-fetched; however, his adherence, though far-fetched, does not entail an impossibility. As for adhering to that regarding God Almighty, it is impossible because it necessitates the assertion of a multiplicity of gods, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof that God is not a body. It is a complex argument based on the impossibility of divine attributes inhering in a composite body. The proof proceeds by considering two possibilities: either God is a single part of the body (the indivisible atom size) or He is the whole composite. The first possibility is rejected because it reduces God to the size of an atom. The second possibility is analyzed into three sub-possibilities: (1) a single attribute subsists in all parts simultaneously—this is impossible because an attribute cannot be in multiple locations; (2) the attribute is divided among the parts—impossible because knowledge is indivisible; (3) each part has its own separate knowledge and power—this leads to multiple gods, which is also impossible. Then an objection is raised: the same reasoning would apply to humans, leading to multiple knowers in one body. The answer distinguishes: philosophers attribute knowledge to the soul, not the body; the Ash‛arites accept that each part of a human is knowing but consider it far-fetched yet not impossible; but applying that to God would imply multiplicity of gods, which is impossible.

Key Terms
متحيز
جوهر فرد
الموصوف
الصفة الواحدة
توزع
تعدد الآلهة
النفس
الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection from human bodies shows that God could also be a body because humans have composite bodies yet are one knower.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the human case is different: either the soul is the knower (philosophers) or each part is knowing but that does not imply multiple gods (Ash'ari). For God, attributing separate knowledge and power to each part would imply multiple gods, an absolute impossibility.

Checkpoint
Why is the possibility that the single attribute is divided among the parts rejected?
A) Because a body cannot have parts.
B) Because knowledge is indivisible.
C) Because it would make God composed of atoms.
D) Because it leads to multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
God's oneness and simplicity preclude any composition that would necessitate multiple divine attributes or multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to imagine that God has parts, how would that affect your trust in His absolute oneness and your worship? Are there any areas of your life where you subconsciously treat God as composed or limited? What will you do to remind yourself of His transcendence today?
?
The proof shows that even one attribute cannot be in multiple parts. How does this reinforce the uniqueness of God's knowledge and power? Do you sometimes attribute equal knowledge to creatures? How can you practically affirm that only God has perfect knowledge?
?
Sanusi states that applying the human analogy to God would lead to multiple gods. How does this challenge any tendency you have to compare God to creation? When you find yourself thinking of God in human terms, what specific step will you take to correct that?

The Argument Against God Being a Body Due to Composition
The author refutes the possibility of God being a body by analyzing composition of parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes God being a body by showing that composition leads to either polytheism or absurdities.

Core Idea
If God were a body, His attributes would lead to multiple deities or absurdities.

The author presents a rigorous argument to prove that God cannot be a body. The reasoning is based on the implications of composition: if God were composed of parts, then either a single part or the whole must possess His attributes. Each possibility is examined and refuted, leading to the conclusion that God must be non-corporeal. This passage also addresses a potential objection from human composition, showing why the same reasoning does not apply to humans in the same way.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The argument begins with the premise that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts. This is the foundational assumption that leads to the subsequent analysis.
The author assumes that body necessarily implies composition.
2
Explore the two main branches
From composition, two possibilities arise: either the divine attributes belong to a single part of the body, or they belong to the whole composite. Identify these two branches in the text.
3
Analyze the three sub-options under the whole
If attributes belong to the whole, there are three sub-possibilities: attributes subsist in all parts together, attributes are divided among parts, or each part has its own complete set of attributes. Notice how each is refuted.
Pay attention to the reasons given for each refutation.
4
Consider the objection and response
An objection is raised that the same reasoning would apply to a human body, leading to absurdity. The author responds by distinguishing between humans and God, citing philosophers and al-Ash'ari's position.
The response shows why the argument does not lead to the same conclusion for humans.

Source Text

العقلاء بأنه في حجم الجوهر الفرد ، وإذا كان جسماً كان مركباً من الأجزاء ، فإما أن يكون الموصوف بالعلم والقدرة والصفات المعتبرة في الإلهية : جزءاً واحداً من تلك الأجزاء ، وإما أن يكون الموصوف بتلك الصفات مجموع تلك الأجزاء . فإن كان الأول كان الإله هو ذلك الجزء الواحد ، منفرداً (۱) فيعود الأمر إلى ما ذكرناه من أن الإله يكون في حجم الجوهر الفرد ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : إما أن تقوم الصفة الواحدة بجميع تلك الأجزاء ، وإما أن تتوزع أجزاء تلك الصفة على تلك الأجزاء ، وإما أن يقوم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علم على حدة وقدرة على حدة ، والأول باطل . لأن قيام الصفة الواحدة بالمحال الكثيرة غير معقول والثاني محال لأن كون العلم قابلا للقسمة محال على ما بيناه في مسألة إثبات النفس. والثالث أيضاً محال ، لأنه يلزم كون كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء موصوفاً بجملة الصفات المعتبرة في الإلهية . وذلك يوجب تعدد الآلهة، وذلك محال فإن قيل : ما ذكرتموه من الدليل قائم في الانسان، فإن مجموع بدنه لا شك أنه مركب من الأجزاء الكثيرة ، فيلزم أن يقوم بكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء علم على حدة ، وقدرة على حدة ، فيلزم أن يكون الإنسان الواحد علماء ، قادرين كثيرين. وذلك باطل . قلنا : أما الفلاسفة فقد طردوا قولهم في الكل وزعموا : أن الموصوف بالعلم والقدرة هو النفس لا الجسم ، وإلا لزم هذا المحال · وأما الأشعري فإنه التزم كون كل واحد من أجزاء الإنسان عالماً قادراً ، وذلك في غاية البعد ، إلا أن التزامه وإن كان بعيداً ، لكن لا يلزم منه محال ، أما التزام ذلك في حق الله تعالى ، فهو محال . لأنه يوجب القول بتعدد الآلهة . [ وهو محال (۲) ] .
The rational [people] [say] that [if He were] in the size of the indivisible atom, and if He were a body, He would be composed of parts. Then either the one described with knowledge, power, and the attributes considered in divinity is a single part of those parts, or the one described with those attributes is the totality of those parts. If the first, then the deity would be that single part, isolated, and the matter returns to what we mentioned that the deity would be in the size of the indivisible atom. And if the second, we say: either the single attribute subsists in all those parts, or parts of that attribute are distributed among those parts, or in each of those parts subsists knowledge separately and power separately. The first is invalid because the subsistence of a single attribute in multiple loci is unintelligible. The second is impossible because knowledge being divisible is impossible as we explained in the question of establishing the soul. The third is also impossible because it would necessitate each of those parts being described with all the attributes considered in divinity, and that would necessitate multiple deities, which is impossible. If it is said: what you have mentioned of the proof applies to man, for the totality of his body is undoubtedly composed of many parts, and it would necessitate that in each of those parts there subsists knowledge separately and power separately, so that one man would be many knowers, many powerful, and that is false. We say: as for the philosophers, they applied their statement consistently and claimed that the one described with knowledge and power is the soul, not the body, otherwise this impossibility would follow. And as for al-Ash'ari, he committed to each part of man being knowing and powerful, which is extremely remote, but his commitment, though remote, does not entail an impossibility. However, committing that regarding God Almighty is impossible because it necessitates saying multiple deities, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument refuting the notion that God could be a body, specifically focusing on the impossibility of composition. The author begins with the premise that if God were a body, He would be composed of parts. Then two main possibilities are considered: either the attributes of divinity (knowledge, power, etc.) belong to a single part, or they belong to the whole composite. The first possibility is dismissed because it would reduce God to an indivisible atom, which is too small and unworthy. The second possibility is then examined through three sub-possibilities: (a) the attributes subsist in all parts collectively, (b) the attributes are divided among the parts, or (c) each part has its own separate knowledge and power. The author refutes (a) by saying a single attribute cannot subsist in multiple loci; (b) because knowledge is indivisible; and (c) because each part would then be fully divine, leading to multiple gods. An objection is raised: this same reasoning would apply to a human body, leading to absurdity. The author answers by noting that philosophers avoid this by attributing knowledge to the soul, not the body. Al-Ash'ari, however, accepts each human part as knowing and powerful—odd but not impossible. However, applying this to God leads to the impossibility of multiple deities. Thus the argument concludes that God cannot be a composite body.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard) - indivisible atom
جزء (juz') - part
جسم (jism) - body
صفة (sifa) - attribute
إلهية (ilahiyya) - divinity
علم ('ilm) - knowledge
قدرة (qudra) - power
محال (muhal) - impossible
نفس (nafs) - soul
أشعري (Ash'ari) - follower of al-Ash'ari

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God were a body, His attributes could be distributed among parts without leading to multiple gods.
Correction: The argument shows that distribution leads to each part having full divinity, which necessitates multiple deities—an impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is the consequence if each part of God's supposed body had its own knowledge and power?
A) God would be a single indivisible atom.
B) The attributes would be divided, making God less powerful.
C) Each part would be a separate deity, leading to polytheism.
D) The body would be meaningless.

Key Takeaway
The argument proves that God cannot be a body because any composition would either make Him unworthy or lead to multiple gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The argument shows that if God were a body, He would either be unworthy or lead to multiple gods. Have you ever, in your heart, imagined God in physical terms, even vaguely? How does this logical demolition confront your assumptions? What concrete step will you take today to purify your concept of God from any trace of anthropomorphism?
?
The objection about the human body being composed of many parts is answered by noting that the soul is the true knower. Do you live as though your body is separate from your soul, or do you conflate the two? How might realizing that your soul is the seat of knowledge and will transform the way you approach worship and repentance?

The Dilemma of Motion for a Bodily God
The author presents the first half of the seventh proof: the dilemma and the first horn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that if God were a body, then either motion is possible or not, and why allowing motion leads to the absurdity that celestial bodies could be gods.

Core Idea
If a moving body can be God, then the sun and moon qualify as gods.

The author presents a powerful dilemma: if God were a body, motion would either be possible for Him or not. This lesson examines the first horn—that motion is possible—and shows it leads to the absurd conclusion that the sun, moon, and even the Throne could be worshiped as gods, which is clear unbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening dilemma
Read the first sentence of the passage carefully. Note the two-part division and the conclusion that both are false.
2
Understand the first horn
Focus on the argument: if motion is possible for a bodily God, then why not the sun and moon? The author lists three defects that prevent them from being gods.
The three defects are composition, finitude, and susceptibility to motion/rest.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
If these defects are not barriers, then worshiping celestial bodies becomes valid—which is atheism. This refutes the first horn.
The argument shows the absurdity of allowing motion for a bodily God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : لو كان جسماً لكان إما أن تكون الحركة جائزة عليه ، وإما أن لا تكون . والقسمان باطلان فالقول بكونه متحيزاً باطل . بيان أن الحركة ممتنعة عليه : أنه لو جاز في الجسم الذي تصح الحركة عليه أن يكون إلها فلم لا يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو الشمس والقمر ؟ لأن الأفلاك والكواكب ليس فيها عيب ، يمنع من كونها آلهة ، إلا أموراً ثلاثة ، وهي كونها مركبة من الأجزاء ، وكونها محدودة متناهية، وكونها قابلة للحركة والسكون . وإذا لم تكن هذه الأشياء مانعة من الإلهية ، فكيف يمكن الطعن في الهية الشمس والقمر ؟ بل في إلهية العرش والكرسي . وذلك عين الكفر والإلحاد، وإنكار الصانع. وأما القسم الثاني وهو أن يقال : إن إله العالم جسم . ولكن الانتقال والحركة عليه محال . فنقول : هذا باطل من وجوه : الأول : إن هذا يكون كالزمن المقعد ، الذي لا يقدر على الحركة وذلك نقص وهو على الله محال . والثاني : إنه تعالى لما كان جسماً ، كان مثلاً لسائر الأجسام ، فكانت الحركة جائزة عليه . والثالث : إن القائلين بكونه جسماً مؤلفاً من الأجزاء والأبعاض ، لا يمتنعون من تجويز الحركة عليه . فإنهم يصفونه تعالى بالذهاب والمجيء ، فتارة يقولون : إنه جالس على العرش ، وقدماه على الكرسي، وهذا هو السكون . وتارة يقولون : إنه ينزل إلى السماء الدنيا وهذا هو الحركة . فهذا جملة الدلائل الدالة على أنه تعالى ليس بجسم . والله أعلم
The seventh proof: If He were a body, then either motion would be permissible for Him, or it would not be. Both divisions are invalid, so the statement that He is spatially extended is invalid. Explanation that motion is impossible for Him: If it were permissible for a body upon which motion is valid to be a god, then why would it not be permissible for the god of the world to be the sun and the moon? Because the celestial spheres and planets have no defect that prevents them from being gods except three things: their being composed of parts, their being finite and limited, and their being subject to motion and rest. And if these things are not preventing divinity, then how can one object to the divinity of the sun and moon? Rather, to the divinity of the Throne and the Footstool? That is pure unbelief and atheism, and denial of the Creator. As for the second division—that it is said: the god of the world is a body, but transition and motion are impossible for Him—we say: this is invalid from several aspects: First: this would make Him like a paralyzed person who cannot move, which is a deficiency, and that is impossible for God. Second: since He, exalted, is a body, He would be similar to other bodies, so motion would be permissible for Him. Third: those who say He is a body composed of parts and limbs do not refrain from allowing motion for Him. For they describe Him, exalted, as going and coming; sometimes they say He is seated on the Throne with His feet on the Footstool—this is rest—and sometimes they say He descends to the lowest heaven—this is motion. This is the sum of the proofs indicating that He, exalted, is not a body. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against the corporeality of God, structured as a dilemma: if God were a body, either motion is possible for Him or it is not; both lead to absurdity. First, the author explains why motion is impossible: if a moving body could be a god, then the sun, moon, and celestial bodies could be considered gods, since they possess only three defects (composition, finitude, and motion/rest) which, if these are not bars to divinity, would allow their worship—which is clear unbelief. Second, if one claims God is a body but motion is impossible for Him, three refutations are given: (1) this would make Him like a paralyzed being, a deficiency; (2) as a body, He would be similar to other bodies and thus subject to motion; (3) actual corporealist sects describe God as moving (descending, coming) and resting (sitting), so they themselves allow motion. Therefore, the only consistent conclusion is that God is not a body at all. The passage ends by stating this is the summary of all proofs.

Key Terms
حركة (haraka) - motion
جسم (jism) - body
متحيز (mutahayyiz) - spatially extended
فلك (falak) - celestial sphere
كوكب (kawkab) - planet/star
متناهي (mutanahi) - finite
نقص (naqs) - deficiency
إلهية (ilahiyya) - divinity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about whether motion is possible for God.
Correction: The argument is not about motion per se, but about the implications of allowing motion for a bodily God: it leads to the absurdity that celestial bodies could be gods.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what are the three defects that prevent the sun and moon from being considered gods?
A) They are not eternal, not all-powerful, and not all-knowing.
B) They are composed of parts, finite, and subject to motion and rest.
C) They are too far away, too hot, and too bright.
D) They are worshipped by pagans, which is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Allowing motion for a bodily God would mean celestial bodies like the sun and moon could also be gods, which is absurd and leads to unbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever unconsciously imagine God as being 'somewhere' or moving? How does this lesson challenge that assumption in your heart?
?
If you were to defend the belief that God is not a body, how would you answer someone who says 'But God sits on the Throne' (Quran 20:5) in a literal sense? What does this proof compel you to reconsider about your own interpretation?
?
The sun and moon are magnificent creations, yet this lesson says that attributing divinity to them is the same as attributing it to any body. Where in your life do you elevate something created—status, wealth, relationships—to a place that only God should occupy? What step will you take today to dethrone that idol?

Refuting a Motionless Bodily God
The author presents three refutations of the claim that God is a motionless body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three refutations against the claim that God is a body but motionless, and how each leads to absurdity or contradiction.

Core Idea
A motionless body is like a paralyzed being—a deficiency impossible for God.

In this lesson, we examine the second horn of the dilemma: the claim that God is a body but cannot move. The author provides three objections: it implies deficiency, it opens the door to motion since all bodies are similar, and it contradicts the actual beliefs of corporealist sects who attribute motion to God. These show the only consistent view is that God is not a body at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the second half of the passage
Read from 'وأما القسم الثاني' to the end. Identify the three objections the author raises.
2
Analyze each objection
First: a motionless body resembles a paralyzed person, a deficiency. Second: if God is a body, He is like other bodies, so motion is possible. Third: actual corporealists describe God as moving (descending) and resting (sitting), so they allow motion.
Each objection undermines the claim that God is a body but motionless.
3
Connect to the overall proof
Understand that both horns of the dilemma are refuted, so the only consistent position is that God is not a body.
The passage concludes that this is the sum of all proofs.

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against the corporeality of God, structured as a dilemma: if God were a body, either motion is possible for Him or it is not; both lead to absurdity. First, the author explains why motion is impossible: if a moving body could be a god, then the sun, moon, and celestial bodies could be considered gods, since they possess only three defects (composition, finitude, and motion/rest) which, if these are not bars to divinity, would allow their worship—which is clear unbelief. سكون (sukun) - rest

Misconception: It is acceptable to say God is a body but does not move.

What is the first refutation given against the claim that God is a motionless body?

D) It implies God is not all-powerful.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God is a body but motionless is refuted by three arguments: it implies deficiency, it allows motion by similarity, and it contradicts corporealist teachings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God in a way that limits Him, even unintentionally? What does this lesson's warning against a motionless body teach you about the subtle ways we might reduce God to a finite object? Will you consciously purify your conception of God from any hint of physical limitation starting today?
?
When you read that a motionless body is like a paralyzed person, what emotional or spiritual reaction does that comparison stir in you? Does it make you uncomfortable to think that any belief about God, even one that seems respectful (like denying motion), could actually be an insult of deficiency? How will you guard your heart from ever attributing to God what is less than His perfection?
?
If someone told you that God sits on a Throne and descends to a lower heaven, and you accepted those descriptions literally, would you be falling into the same inconsistency as the groups refuted here? How can you ensure that your understanding of God's actions never degrades into imagining Him as a being that moves or rests like a creature?

Refuting the Claim That God is a Body
Refutes the claim that God could be a celestial body or a motionless body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments against considering God a body, including why celestial bodies cannot be divine and why a motionless body is impossible for God.

Core Idea
God cannot be a body because bodies are limited, composite, and subject to motion or rest.

In this passage, the author presents two key arguments against the belief that God is a body. First, he addresses the possibility that a celestial body like the sun or moon could be divine, showing that such a claim leads to absurdity. Then, he refutes the idea that God could be a body that does not move, demonstrating that such a notion implies imperfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's structure emerge.
The passage has two main arguments.
2
Identify the two parts
Notice that the passage starts with 'Why is it not permissible...' for the first argument, then 'As for the second part...' for the second.
3
Analyze the three defects
List the three defects that prevent celestial bodies from being gods: composed of parts, finite, subject to motion/rest.
The author argues that if these don't prevent divinity, then anything could be claimed divine, leading to disbelief.
4
Examine the three objections
For the second argument, note the three objections: a motionless body is like a paralytic (deficiency), a body would be like others thus motion is possible, and proponents themselves describe God moving and resting.
These show inconsistency and impossibility.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the totality of proofs indicates God is not a body. Consider how these arguments affirm divine transcendence.
